Let's try to derive using complex numbers or coordinates but with a more clever choice: Use coordinate system with origin at M, and let the common chord be the y-axis. That is what we have: M = (0,0), N = (d,0), A = (x0, y0), B = (x0, -y0). We already have nice expressions: y_P = -p (x0+r)/y0, and H = (p, - (d-p) y0/(x0+r)). Also, v = (p-x0, y_P-y0). Compute v more explicitly.

We have v = (p - x0, y_P - y0) = (p - x0, -p (x0+r)/y0 - y0) = (p - x0, -(p (x0+r) + y0^2)/y0). And y0^2 = r^2 - x0^2.

So p (x0+r) + y0^2 = p(x0+r) + r^2 - x0^2 = p(x0+r) + (r-x0)(r+x0) = (x0+r)(p + r - x0). So v = (p - x0, -(x0+r)(p + r - x0)/y0).

Thus v = (p - x0, -(x0+r)(p + r - x0)/y0).

Now note that p + r - x0 =? p = (d+R-r)/2, so p+r = (d+R+r)/2. So p+r-x0 = (d+R+r)/2 - x0. Not sure.

But we can also compute the direction vector of AP: from A to P: (p-x0, y_P - y0). So that's v. So line ℓ through H with direction v.

Now we need to show ℓ is tangent to circle (BEF). So let's compute B, E, F.

B = (x0, -y0).

Now E = A + t_E v, where t_E = -2(A·v)/|v|^2.

A·v = (x0, y0) · (p-x0, y_P - y0) = x0(p-x0) + y0(y_P - y0) = x0(p-x0) + y0 y_P - y0^2.

But y0 y_P = y0 * (-p (x0+r)/y0) = -p (x0+r). So A·v = x0(p-x0) - p(x0+r) - y0^2 = x0 p - x0^2 - p x0 - p r - y0^2 = -x0^2 - p r - y0^2 = -(x0^2+y0^2) - p r = -r^2 - p r = -r(r+p). So A·v = -r(p+r). Good simplification!

Thus t_E = -2(-r(p+r))/|v|^2 = 2r(p+r)/|v|^2.

Similarly, (A-N)·v: A-N = (x0-d, y0). Then (A-N)·v = (x0-d)(p-x0) + y0(y_P - y0) = (x0-d)(p-x0) + y0 y_P - y0^2. y0 y_P = -p(x0+r). So (A-N)·v = (x0-d)(p-x0) - p(x0+r) - y0^2 = Expand: (x0-d)(p-x0) = (x0-d)p - (x0-d)x0 = p x0 - p d - x0^2 + d x0. Then subtract p(x0+r) gives: p x0 - p d - x0^2 + d x0 - p x0 - p r = -p d - x0^2 + d x0 - p r. Then subtract y0^2: = -p d - x0^2 + d x0 - p r - (r^2 - x0^2) = -p d - x0^2 + d x0 - p r - r^2 + x0^2 = -p d + d x0 - p r - r^2 = d(x0 - p) - r(p+r). But x0-p = -(p-x0). So d(x0-p) = -d(p-x0). So (A-N)·v = -d(p-x0) - r(p+r). So (A-N)·v = -[d(p-x0) + r(p+r)]. Then t_F = -2((A-N)·v)/|v|^2 = -2(-[d(p-x0) + r(p+r)])/|v|^2 = 2(d(p-x0) + r(p+r))/|v|^2.

Thus we have:

E = A + (2r(p+r)/|v|^2) v
F = A + (2(d(p-x0) + r(p+r))/|v|^2) v

So both E and F are on line AP, as expected.

Now we need to find circle through B, E, F. That is a circle in the plane. Since B is the reflection of A across x-axis, and E, F lie on line AP, perhaps the circle (BEF) is symmetric in some way.

Let’s denote the line AP: param t with point X(t) = A + t v. Then A corresponds to t=0, P to t=1, E to t_E = 2r(p+r)/|v|^2, F to t_F = 2(d(p-x0) + r(p+r))/|v|^2.

Now B = (x0, -y0). Note that B is not on line AP generally.

We need to prove that line ℓ through H parallel to AP (direction v) is tangent to circle (BEF). Equivalent: The distance from the center of circle (BEF) to line ℓ equals its radius. Since ℓ has direction v, it is parallel to AP. So it's essentially a line with direction v.

Alternatively, perhaps the circle (BEF) has a property that its center lies on the line through H perpendicular to AP? That would make the tangent line through H parallel to AP: if the center is on a line perpendicular to AP, then the line through H parallel to AP is tangent if and only if H is the point of tangency? No, if a line is tangent to a circle, the radius to the point of tangency is perpendicular to the line. So if we want a line through H parallel to AP to be tangent, then the radius from the center O to the point of tangency T is perpendicular to AP. So O lies on the line through T perpendicular to AP. Since T is on the line ℓ, and ℓ is parallel to AP, the perpendicular from O to ℓ is the line through O perpendicular to AP. So O is on a line perpendicular to AP. The foot of the perpendicular from O to ℓ is the tangency point. So if we can show that the center O of circle (BEF) lies on the line through H perpendicular to AP, and also that the distance from H to O equals the distance from O to the line ℓ? Actually for a line through H parallel to AP to be tangent, we need that the distance from O to the line equals the radius, and also that the line passes through H. But H is a specific point; the line ℓ is determined by H and direction v. So we need to show that ℓ is tangent, i.e., that the line ℓ touches the circle. It could be that ℓ is tangent at a point different from H; H may not be the tangency point. So we need to show that the distance from O to ℓ equals the radius.

Maybe we can compute the circle equation using coordinates, then compute the distance from O to ℓ, and show it equals the radius. This is algebraically heavy but perhaps manageable with simplifications using the known relations.

Let's proceed with analytic geometry.

So, let's set up coordinates:

A = (0,0). Let AP be x-axis. So P = (p,0) with p>0 (since P is on line AP; we can choose orientation so that P is to the right of A). Then E = (e,0) and F = (f,0) with e and f determined by the circles.

Let M = (α, β). Then Ω: x^2+y^2 = 2α x + 2β y (since passes through origin). The radius squared r^2 = α^2+β^2.

Let N = (γ, δ). Then Γ: x^2+y^2 = 2γ x + 2δ y. Radius squared R^2 = γ^2+δ^2.

We are given that r < R, so α^2+β^2 < γ^2+δ^2.

Also, the circles intersect at A=0 and B. The radical axis is line through A and B: (α-γ)x+(β-δ)y=0. So B is on that line. The line of centers MN: through M and N. Let's denote its equation.

Now, the points C and D are on MN and on the circles, with order C, M, N, D. So M is between C and N, and N is between M and D. So we can find C as the intersection of MN with Ω on the side opposite to N from M. Similarly, D is intersection of MN with Γ on the side opposite to M from N.

We can parameterize line MN. Let direction vector d = N - M = (γ-α, δ-β). Then any point on MN: M + t d, with t real. At t=0 we get M, t=1 we get N. Then C is on Ω and corresponds to some t_C < 0 (since M is between C and N, C is on the opposite side of M from N). D is on Γ and corresponds to some t_D > 1 (since N is between M and D). So |t_C d| = r => |t_C| |d| = r. Since t_C < 0, we have t_C = - r/|d|. Similarly, for D = M + t_D d, we need |D - N| = R. Since N = M + d, so D - N = (M + t_D d) - (M + d) = (t_D - 1) d. So |(t_D - 1) d| = R => |t_D - 1| |d| = R. Since t_D > 1, we have t_D - 1 = R/|d| => t_D = 1 + R/|d|.

Thus we have:

C = M - (r/|d|) d
D = M + (1 + R/|d|) d

But note: d = N - M. So C = M - (r/|d|) (N-M). D = M + (1 + R/|d|)(N-M).

Now, we also have P, the circumcenter of triangle ACD. Since A=0, C, D are known. The circumcenter P is the intersection of perpendicular bisectors of AC and AD (or CD). We can compute P using formulas.

Let’s denote:

C = (c_x, c_y), D = (d_x, d_y). A=(0,0). Then P is the point such that |P| = |P-C| = |P-D|. This is the center of the circle through origin, C, D. So P is the circumcenter of triangle O C D.

We can find P using the fact that it is the intersection of perpendicular bisectors of OC and OD. The perpendicular bisector of OC is the set of points X such that X·C = |C|^2/2. Similarly, for OD: X·D = |D|^2/2. So P satisfies:

P·C = |C|^2/2
P·D = |D|^2/2

Thus P is the solution to these linear equations. In 2D, if C and D are not collinear (they are not, because they are on line MN, but they are distinct and not collinear with origin? Actually C and D are on line MN; the origin is not necessarily on that line. So C and D are two vectors; the equations define P uniquely if C and D are linearly independent? They are not necessarily independent; they are collinear with each other (since they lie on same line). Actually C and D are on the same line through M and N; but the origin is not on that line generally. So vectors C and D are not necessarily collinear; they are points, but as vectors from origin, they are not necessarily collinear because the line MN does not necessarily pass through origin. So C and D are two distinct points not on the same line through origin? Actually they lie on line MN, which is a line not passing through origin generally. So the vectors C and D are not scalar multiples of each other; they are just points on a line not through origin. So they are linearly independent (unless the line passes through origin). So the system P·C = |C|^2/2 and P·D = |D|^2/2 has a unique solution if C and D are not parallel? Actually if C and D are linearly independent, then the matrix with rows C^T and D^T is invertible. So P is unique. So we can compute P by solving linear equations.

Then H is orthocenter of triangle PMN. Since M and N are known, P is known. We can compute H using the formula: In a triangle, orthocenter H = M + N + P - 2 O? Not exactly. Not simple. But we can compute H as intersection of altitudes. Since we have coordinates, we can compute H using the fact that H = (p + m + n) - 2 (circumcenter)? Actually there is relation: For any triangle, the orthocenter H is given by H = A + B + C - 2 O, where O is circumcenter (if using vector positions with origin at circumcenter? Wait, in Euclidean geometry, if we place the circumcenter at the origin, then the vertices are vectors on a circle, and the orthocenter is the sum of the vertices. More generally, if we denote vectors from any origin, then H = A + B + C - 2O, where O is circumcenter. Because if we shift origin to O, then A' = A-O, etc., and H' = A'+B'+C', so H = O + (A-O)+(B-O)+(C-O)= A+B+C-2O. Yes, that's correct. However, we might not need explicit H; we need to show that the horizontal line through H is tangent to circle (BEF). Since AP is x-axis, H has some y-coordinate y_H. So we need to show that the circle through B, E, F has a horizontal tangent line at y = y_H. So if we can compute the circumcenter O_BEF and radius, and show that y_H = o_y ± R, we are done.

Now, E and F are on x-axis: E = (e,0), F = (f,0). Since A=0, and the circles pass through A, the second intersections of x-axis with circles are given by solving x^2 = 2α x + 2β *0? Actually for Ω: x^2 = 2α x + 2β y. On x-axis, y=0, so x^2 = 2α x => x(x-2α)=0. So the roots are x=0 (A) and x=2α. So e = 2α. Similarly, for Γ: x^2 = 2γ x => x=0 or x=2γ, so f = 2γ. Thus E = (2α, 0), F = (2γ, 0). Good.

Now, B is the other intersection of the circles. Since A=0, B lies on the radical axis: (α-γ)x+(β-δ)y=0. Also, B lies on both circles. We can find B coordinates by solving the system: x^2+y^2 = 2α x+2β y and x^2+y^2 = 2γ x+2δ y. Subtract: (α-γ)x+(β-δ)y=0 => line through origin. So B is the second intersection of that line with either circle. So we can parameterize B = t * (β-δ, -(α-γ))? Actually the line equation: (α-γ)x+(β-δ)y=0 => direction vector perpendicular to (α-γ, β-δ) is (β-δ, -(α-γ)). So B = k * (β-δ, -(α-γ)) for some k. Since A=0, B is some scalar multiple of that vector. Then we can find k by plugging into one circle equation.

Let’s denote:

Let u = α-γ, v = β-δ. Then line AB: u x + v y = 0. So direction vector: (v, -u). So B = t (v, -u) for some t ≠ 0. Then B lies on Ω: |B|^2 = 2α B_x + 2β B_y. Compute:

|B|^2 = t^2 (v^2+u^2). B_x = t v, B_y = -t u.
RHS: 2α t v + 2β (-t u) = 2t (α v - β u). So equation: t^2 (u^2+v^2) = 2t (α v - β u). Since t ≠ 0, we have t (u^2+v^2) = 2 (α v - β u). So t = 2 (α v - β u)/(u^2+v^2). Note that u^2+v^2 = |(α-γ, β-δ)|^2 = |N-M|^2 = |d|^2.

Thus B = (2 (α v - β u) / |d|^2) * (v, -u). Alternatively, we can compute B directly.

Now, we have points:

B (some coordinates)
E = (2α, 0)
F = (2γ, 0)

We need to find the circumcircle of triangle BEF. Since E and F lie on x-axis, the perpendicular bisector of EF is vertical line x = (2α+2γ)/2 = α+γ. So the circumcenter O = (α+γ, o_y) for some o_y. And radius R = distance from O to E = sqrt((α+γ-2α)^2 + o_y^2) = sqrt((γ-α)^2 + o_y^2). Also, O must satisfy distance to B equals R: |O - B|^2 = (γ-α)^2 + o_y^2.

Thus we can find o_y from equation:

(O_x - B_x)^2 + (O_y - B_y)^2 = (γ-α)^2 + o_y^2.

But O_x = α+γ, O_y = o_y. So:

(α+γ - B_x)^2 + (o_y - B_y)^2 = (γ-α)^2 + o_y^2.

Expand: (α+γ - B_x)^2 + o_y^2 - 2 o_y B_y + B_y^2 = (γ-α)^2 + o_y^2.

Cancel o_y^2: (α+γ - B_x)^2 - 2 o_y B_y + B_y^2 = (γ-α)^2.

Thus solve for o_y:

2 o_y B_y = (α+γ - B_x)^2 + B_y^2 - (γ-α)^2.

So o_y = [ (α+γ - B_x)^2 + B_y^2 - (γ-α)^2 ] / (2 B_y), provided B_y ≠ 0.

Now, we also need y_H, the y-coordinate of H (orthocenter of triangle PMN). Then the condition that the horizontal line y = y_H is tangent to circle (BEF) is that y_H = o_y ± R, i.e., (y_H - o_y)^2 = R^2 = (γ-α)^2 + o_y^2.

So we need to verify that (y_H - o_y)^2 = (γ-α)^2 + o_y^2.

This is equivalent to y_H^2 - 2 y_H o_y + o_y^2 = (γ-α)^2 + o_y^2 => y_H^2 - 2 y_H o_y = (γ-α)^2 => 2 y_H o_y = y_H^2 - (γ-α)^2 => o_y = (y_H^2 - (γ-α)^2)/(2 y_H), provided y_H ≠ 0.

Thus if we can compute y_H and show that it satisfies that relation with o_y, we are done. So we need to find y_H in terms of α,β,γ,δ. And also compute o_y from B.

So we need to compute P, then H.

Let's compute C and D explicitly in terms of α,β,γ,δ.

We have M = (α,β), N = (γ,δ). d = N - M = (γ-α, δ-β). |d| = sqrt((γ-α)^2+(δ-β)^2). Denote L = |d|.

Then:

C = M - (r/L) d, where r = sqrt(α^2+β^2). But note: r = sqrt(α^2+β^2) is the distance from M to A (origin). So r = |M|.

Thus C = (α,β) - (|M|/L) (γ-α, δ-β) = (α - (|M|/L)(γ-α), β - (|M|/L)(δ-β)).

Similarly, D = M + (1 + R/L) d, where R = |N| = sqrt(γ^2+δ^2). So D = (α,β) + (1 + |N|/L)(γ-α, δ-β) = (α + (1+|N|/L)(γ-α), β + (1+|N|/L)(δ-β)).

Now, P is circumcenter of triangle ACD with A=0. As derived, P satisfies:

P·C = |C|^2/2
P·D = |D|^2/2

We can solve for P. This is a linear system in P. Write P = (p_x, p_y). Then:

p_x C_x + p_y C_y = (C_x^2+C_y^2)/2
p_x D_x + p_y D_y = (D_x^2+D_y^2)/2

We can solve using matrix inversion. However, maybe there is a simpler geometric interpretation: Since A=0, the circumcenter P is the center of the circle through origin, C, D. There is known formula: In complex numbers, if we treat points as complex numbers, the circumcenter of triangle with vertices 0, c, d is given by (c d (c̄ - d̄)) / (c d̄ - c̄ d)? Not exactly. But we can compute.

Similarly, |D|^2 = |M + t d|^2 = r^2 + 2t M·d + t^2 L^2.

P·(M + t d) = (r^2 + 2t M·d + t^2 L^2)/2

Subtract: P·((t-s)d) = (2t M·d - 2s M·d + (t^2 - s^2)L^2)/2 = ((t-s)2M·d + (t-s)(t+s)L^2)/2 = (t-s)(2M·d + (t+s)L^2)/2.

Since t-s = (1+R/L) - (-r/L) = 1 + (R+r)/L. So t-s ≠ 0. So we get:

P·d = (2M·d + (t+s)L^2)/2.

Now, we also have from the first equation: P·M + P·(s d) = (r^2 + 2s M·d + s^2 L^2)/2. So P·M = (r^2 + 2s M·d + s^2 L^2)/2 - s P·d.

Plug s = -r/L. Then we can solve for P·M.

Given the time, I'll try to complete the analytic proof using the coordinate system with MN as x-axis, and compute O_BEF and then verify the tangency condition. This is heavy but we can use the simplifications we already have. Let's proceed.

We have:

M = (0,0)
N = (d,0)
A = (x0, y0)
B = (x0, -y0)
C = (-r, 0)
D = (d+R, 0)
p = (d+R-r)/2
y_P = -p (x0+r)/y0
dx = p - x0
dy = y_P - y0

We have t_E = 2r(p+r)/|v|^2, t_F = 2(d(p-x0)+r(p+r))/|v|^2.

Define |v|^2 = dx^2 + dy^2.

We also have H = (p, y_H) with y_H = - (d-p) y0/(x0+r).

Now, we need to find the circumcenter O of triangle BEF. Let’s denote:

E = (x_E, y_E) = (x0 + t_E dx, y0 + t_E dy)
F = (x_F, y_F) = (x0 + t_F dx, y0 + t_F dy)

Since E and F lie on line L: (x0, y0) + t v.

Now, B = (x0, -y0).

We can compute O. Since E and F are on L, the perpendicular bisector of EF is perpendicular to L at the midpoint of EF. Let’s denote t_mid = (t_E+t_F)/2. Then midpoint M_EF = (x0 + t_mid dx, y0 + t_mid dy). The direction vector of L is v = (dx, dy). A vector perpendicular to v is n = (dy, -dx). So O = M_EF + μ n for some μ.

We also have OB = OE (since O is circumcenter). So |O-B|^2 = |O-E|^2. This gives an equation to solve for μ.

Let’s compute O - B and O - E.

Let’s define:

O = (x0 + t_mid dx + μ dy, y0 + t_mid dy - μ dx).

Then:

O - B = (x0 + t_mid dx + μ dy - x0, y0 + t_mid dy - μ dx + y0) = (t_mid dx + μ dy, 2y0 + t_mid dy - μ dx).

O - E = (x0 + t_mid dx + μ dy - (x0 + t_E dx), y0 + t_mid dy - μ dx - (y0 + t_E dy)) = ((t_mid - t_E) dx + μ dy, (t_mid - t_E) dy - μ dx).

Set |O-B|^2 = |O-E|^2.

Compute:

(t_mid dx + μ dy)^2 + (2y0 + t_mid dy - μ dx)^2 = ((t_mid - t_E) dx + μ dy)^2 + ((t_mid - t_E) dy - μ dx)^2.

Expand both sides. Let’s denote:

LHS = (t_mid dx)^2 + 2 t_mid dx μ dy + μ^2 dy^2 + (2y0)^2 + 2*2y0*(t_mid dy - μ dx) + (t_mid dy - μ dx)^2
= t_mid^2 dx^2 + 2 t_mid dx μ dy + μ^2 dy^2 + 4y0^2 + 4y0 t_mid dy - 4y0 μ dx + (t_mid^2 dy^2 - 2 t_mid dy μ dx + μ^2 dx^2).

LHS = t_mid^2 (dx^2+dy^2) + μ^2 (dx^2+dy^2) + 2 t_mid dx μ dy - 2 t_mid dy μ dx + 2 t_mid dy μ dx? Wait, we have +2 t_mid dx μ dy from first term, and -2 t_mid dy μ dx from the last term? Actually the last term gives -2 t_mid dy μ dx. So combine: 2 t_mid dx μ dy - 2 t_mid dy μ dx = 0? Because dx μ dy = μ dx dy, and dy μ dx = μ dx dy, so they cancel. So LHS = t_mid^2 |v|^2 + μ^2 |v|^2 + 4y0^2 + 4y0 t_mid dy - 4y0 μ dx.

Thus LHS = |v|^2 (t_mid^2 + μ^2) + 4y0^2 + 4y0 t_mid dy - 4y0 μ dx.

Now RHS: ((t_mid - t_E) dx + μ dy)^2 + ((t_mid - t_E) dy - μ dx)^2. Expand:

First term: (t_mid - t_E)^2 dx^2 + 2(t_mid - t_E) dx μ dy + μ^2 dy^2.
Second term: (t_mid - t_E)^2 dy^2 - 2(t_mid - t_E) dy μ dx + μ^2 dx^2.

Sum: (t_mid - t_E)^2 (dx^2+dy^2) + μ^2 (dx^2+dy^2) + 2(t_mid - t_E) μ (dx dy - dy dx) = (t_mid - t_E)^2 |v|^2 + μ^2 |v|^2 + 0. So RHS = |v|^2 ((t_mid - t_E)^2 + μ^2).

Set LHS = RHS:

|v|^2 (t_mid^2 + μ^2) + 4y0^2 + 4y0 t_mid dy - 4y0 μ dx = |v|^2 ((t_mid - t_E)^2 + μ^2).

Cancel μ^2 |v|^2 both sides:

|v|^2 t_mid^2 + 4y0^2 + 4y0 t_mid dy - 4y0 μ dx = |v|^2 (t_mid - t_E)^2.

Thus:

|v|^2 (t_mid^2 - (t_mid - t_E)^2) = -4y0^2 - 4y0 t_mid dy + 4y0 μ dx.

But t_mid^2 - (t_mid - t_E)^2 = t_mid^2 - (t_mid^2 - 2 t_mid t_E + t_E^2) = 2 t_mid t_E - t_E^2 = t_E (2 t_mid - t_E).

So:

|v|^2 t_E (2 t_mid - t_E) = -4y0^2 - 4y0 t_mid dy + 4y0 μ dx.

Thus we can solve for μ:

4y0 μ dx = |v|^2 t_E (2 t_mid - t_E) + 4y0^2 + 4y0 t_mid dy.

So μ = [ |v|^2 t_E (2 t_mid - t_E) + 4y0^2 + 4y0 t_mid dy ] / (4y0 dx).

Now, we also have t_mid = (t_E+t_F)/2. And we have expressions for t_E, t_F in terms of |v|^2. This may simplify.

Let's compute t_E and t_F in terms of |v|^2 and the other parameters.

We have:

t_E = 2r(p+r)/|v|^2.
t_F = 2(d(p-x0)+r(p+r))/|v|^2.

Thus t_E and t_F are known.

Also, p = (d+R-r)/2, x0 = (d^2 - R^2 + r^2)/(2d). We also have y0^2 = r^2 - x0^2.

We might need to compute |v|^2 = dx^2+dy^2. We have dx = p - x0, dy = y_P - y0 = -p(x0+r)/y0 - y0 = -(p(x0+r)+y0^2)/y0. And we have y0^2 = r^2 - x0^2. So p(x0+r)+y0^2 = p(x0+r)+r^2-x0^2 = p(x0+r)+(r-x0)(r+x0) = (x0+r)(p+r-x0). So dy = -(x0+r)(p+r-x0)/y0.

Thus v = (p-x0, -(x0+r)(p+r-x0)/y0). So |v|^2 = (p-x0)^2 + (x0+r)^2 (p+r-x0)^2 / y0^2.

We can also compute t_E (2 t_mid - t_E) = t_E (t_E + t_F - t_E) = t_E t_F. Because 2 t_mid - t_E = (t_E+t_F) - t_E = t_F. So indeed, 2 t_mid - t_E = t_F. So the left side becomes |v|^2 t_E t_F. Great!

Thus we have:

|v|^2 t_E t_F = -4y0^2 - 4y0 t_mid dy + 4y0 μ dx.

So:

4y0 μ dx = |v|^2 t_E t_F + 4y0^2 + 4y0 t_mid dy.

Thus μ = [ |v|^2 t_E t_F + 4y0^2 + 4y0 t_mid dy ] / (4y0 dx). Good.

Now, we need to compute O = (x0 + t_mid dx + μ dy, y0 + t_mid dy - μ dx). Then we need to show that the horizontal line y = y_H is tangent to circle (BEF). But that is equivalent to: distance from O to line ℓ (which is through H with direction v) equals radius. However, we have already a simpler condition: y_H = o_y ± R. But we can compute o_y and R from O. Since O = (o_x, o_y) and R = distance to E. Then the horizontal line y = y_H is tangent if (y_H - o_y)^2 = R^2. So we need to compute o_y and R in terms of μ, t_mid, etc.

We can also compute the distance from O to the horizontal line y = y_H as |y_H - o_y|, but that's not directly the distance to ℓ; ℓ is not horizontal. So we need to compute the distance from O to line ℓ: ℓ is through H with direction v. The distance from O to ℓ is |(O-H) × v|/|v|. That should equal R. So we need to verify that |(O-H) × v|^2 = |v|^2 R^2.

Given the complexity, perhaps we can find that O lies on a certain line related to H and v. Maybe O, H, and the center of something are collinear in a way that simplifies the cross product.

Alternatively, we can compute the equation of circle (BEF) directly using coordinates and then compute the condition that the line ℓ is tangent. Since ℓ passes through H and is parallel to v, its equation is: (x - p, y - y_H) is parallel to v, so it is the set of points X such that (X - H) × v = 0? Actually the line ℓ is { X : (X - H) is a scalar multiple of v }. So it's the line with direction v through H. The condition that ℓ is tangent to circle (BEF) means that the quadratic equation in s for the intersection of ℓ and the circle has a double root. That is, the equation |H + s v - O|^2 = R^2 has discriminant zero. So we need to show that (|H-O|^2 - R^2)^2 = |v|^2 |(H-O) · v|^2? Actually, the line ℓ: X = H + s v. Then the intersection with circle: |X-O|^2 = R^2 gives |H-O|^2 + 2s (H-O)·v + s^2|v|^2 = R^2. This is a quadratic in s: |v|^2 s^2 + 2 (H-O)·v s + (|H-O|^2 - R^2)=0. The line is tangent iff this quadratic has a double root, i.e., its discriminant Δ = 4 [(H-O)·v]^2 - 4|v|^2 (|H-O|^2 - R^2)=0, or equivalently, [(H-O)·v]^2 = |v|^2 (|H-O|^2 - R^2). So we need to verify that (H-O)·v = ± |v| sqrt(|H-O|^2 - R^2). Since we are dealing with vectors, we can compute.

Thus we need to compute O and then check this identity.

Given the time, I think a coordinate proof is feasible but we must be systematic. I'll attempt to compute P, H, and then the tangency condition in the A=0, AP=x-axis system. This seems to give simpler expressions for E and F and the circle (BEF). Let's proceed with that.

We have:

A = (0,0)
P = (p,0) with p>0
M = (α,β)
N = (γ,δ)
r = sqrt(α^2+β^2), R = sqrt(γ^2+δ^2)
L = |N-M| = sqrt((γ-α)^2+(δ-β)^2)

We also have:

C = M - (r/L) (N-M) = (α - (r/L)(γ-α), β - (r/L)(δ-β))
D = M + (1+R/L)(N-M) = (α + (1+R/L)(γ-α), β + (1+R/L)(δ-β))

Now, P is circumcenter of triangle ACD. Since A=0, we have P·C = |C|^2/2 and P·D = |D|^2/2. But P = (p,0). So:

p C_x = |C|^2/2  => C_x = |C|^2/(2p)
p D_x = |D|^2/2  => D_x = |D|^2/(2p)

Thus we can compute p from either, and also we have a consistency condition that |C|^2 / C_x = |D|^2 / D_x.

Let's compute C_x and |C|^2 in terms of α,β,γ,δ. Since we have M·d = (R^2 - r^2 - L^2)/2. Also, we can compute C_x = α - (r/L)(γ-α). We also need |C|^2 = C_x^2 + C_y^2. But we can also compute |C|^2 using the formula: |C|^2 = r^2 + 2s M·d + s^2 L^2, with s = -r/L. So |C|^2 = r^2 - (2r/L) M·d + (r^2/L^2)L^2 = r^2 - (2r/L) M·d + r^2 = 2r^2 - (2r/L) M·d. And M·d = (R^2 - r^2 - L^2)/2. So |C|^2 = 2r^2 - (2r/L)*((R^2 - r^2 - L^2)/2) = 2r^2 - (r/L)(R^2 - r^2 - L^2) = 2r^2 - (rR^2)/L + (r^3)/L + rL.

Thus |C|^2 = r(2r + L + (r^2 - R^2)/L). (Equation 1)

Similarly, |D|^2 = R(2R + L + (R^2 - r^2)/L). (Equation 2)

Now, we also have C_x = α - (r/L)(γ-α). We need to relate C_x to the parameters. Note that C is on line MN, and also on circle Ω. There might be an expression for C_x in terms of r, R, L. Now, we also know that C lies on Ω: |C-M| = r. So that gave the relation above. But we might be able to find C_x in terms of α,γ, r, L. However, we also have that P is on x-axis, so we have p = |C|^2/(2C_x). So p = [r(2r + L + (r^2 - R^2)/L)] / [2(α - (r/L)(γ-α))]. That is an expression.

But perhaps we can compute p directly from the condition that P is the circumcenter of ACD. There is a known formula: In complex numbers, if A=0, then the circumcenter of triangle with vertices 0, c, d is given by (c d (c̄ - d̄))/(c d̄ - c̄ d). That is a complex number. If we set that equal to (p,0) (real), then we have:

(c d (c̄ - d̄))/(c d̄ - c̄ d) = p (real).

That gives a relation that might be simpler. But we need to compute c and d as complex numbers.

Let's treat M and N as complex numbers: m = α + iβ, n = γ + iδ. Then d = n - m. L = |d|.

Then c = m - (r/L) d = m - (|m|/L)(n-m) = m - (|m|/L)n + (|m|/L)m = m(1 + |m|/L) - (|m|/L)n.
d = m + (1 + R/L)(n-m) = m + (1+R/L)n - (1+R/L)m = (1 - (1+R/L))m + (1+R/L)n = (-R/L)m + (1+R/L)n.

So c = (1 + r/L)m - (r/L)n, where r = |m|.
d = (1+R/L)n - (R/L)m.

Now, the circumcenter P = (c d (c̄ - d̄))/(c d̄ - c̄ d). Since we know P is on the real axis (x-axis), its imaginary part is 0. So that gives a condition.

But maybe we can compute p directly by solving the linear equations from the circumcenter conditions using vector approach: Since P = (p,0) satisfies P·C = |C|^2/2 and P·D = |D|^2/2. p = |C|^2/(2 C_x) = |D|^2/(2 D_x). So we can compute p if we can express C_x and D_x in terms of r,R,L. However, C_x is not purely a function of r,R,L; it also depends on the coordinates of M and N. But perhaps we can find that C_x = (|C|^2)/(2p) and also we can compute C_x using the fact that C lies on the line MN. There might be a relation between C_x and the x-coordinates of M and N. Now, we need to prove this identity in general. The algebra seems to work.

Let's attempt to derive a general proof using vector algebra with the MN as x-axis system. We have:

Let’s denote:

Let M = (0,0)
N = (d,0)
A = (x0, y0), B = (x0, -y0)
C = (-r, 0)
D = (d+R, 0)
p = (d+R-r)/2
y_P = -p (x0+r)/y0
v = (dx, dy) = (p - x0, y_P - y0)

We also have:

t_E = 2r(p+r)/|v|^2
t_F = 2(d(p-x0)+r(p+r))/|v|^2

We have H = (p, y_H) with y_H = - (d-p) y0/(x0+r)

Now, we need to show that the line through H parallel to v is tangent to circle (BEF). This is equivalent to showing that the quadratic in s for the intersection of line ℓ: H + s v with circle (BEF) has discriminant zero. That is:

|(H - O) + s v|^2 = R^2, where O is circumcenter of BEF, R = |O-B|.

So discriminant condition: |(H-O) × v|^2 = |v|^2 |H-O|^2 - |v|^2 R^2? Actually, the quadratic: |v|^2 s^2 + 2 (H-O)·v s + (|H-O|^2 - R^2)=0. Discriminant Δ = 4[(H-O)·v]^2 - 4|v|^2 (|H-O|^2 - R^2)=0 => [(H-O)·v]^2 = |v|^2 (|H-O|^2 - R^2). So we need to verify this identity.

Alternatively, we can show that the line ℓ is the polar of some point with respect to circle (BEF). That might be easier: The polar of a point X with respect to a circle is the line through the points of tangency of the tangents from X. The condition that a line ℓ is tangent to the circle is equivalent to the fact that the pole of ℓ lies on the circle? Not exactly.

Maybe we can find that the point A is the pole of line ℓ with respect to circle (BEF). In numeric, A=(0.25,0.9682), and circle (BEF) center (1,-1.291), radius 0.816. Distance from A to O = sqrt((0.75)^2+(2.2592)^2)= sqrt(0.5625+5.104)= sqrt(5.6665)=2.38, not equal to radius. So A not on (BEF). So not that.

Given the time, I think it's more feasible to produce a coordinate proof in the MN as x-axis system, but we need to simplify the algebra. Let's denote:

Let’s use the following notations to reduce clutter:

Let r, R, d, x0, y0 be as defined.
Let p = (d+R-r)/2.
Let L = |v|^2 = (p-x0)^2 + (y_P - y0)^2. We have y_P = -p (x0+r)/y0.
Thus we can compute L.

Also, let t_E = 2r(p+r)/L, t_F = 2(d(p-x0)+r(p+r))/L.
Note that t_F = 2(d(p-x0)+r(p+r))/L = 2(d(p-x0))/L + t_E.

We also have B = (x0, -y0), E = A + t_E v, F = A + t_F v.

Now, we can compute the circumcenter O of triangle BEF. Since E and F are on line L: A + t v, and B is symmetric to A across x-axis, maybe we can find a formula for O in terms of A, v, and B. Let's try to compute O using the fact that O is the intersection of perpendicular bisector of EF and perpendicular bisector of BF (or BE). Since E and F are on line L, the perpendicular bisector of EF is the line perpendicular to v through the midpoint of EF. That midpoint is M_EF = A + (t_E+t_F)/2 v = A + t_mid v, where t_mid = (t_E+t_F)/2. So O = A + t_mid v + μ n, where n = (dy, -dx) is a unit vector perpendicular to v (since dot with v = 0). So O = A + t_mid v + μ n.

Now, O must also satisfy that distance to B equals distance to E (or F). Since O is on perpendicular bisector of EF, we have OE = OF. So we only need OB = OE. So:

|O - B|^2 = |O - E|^2.

Compute O - B = (A + t_mid v + μ n) - B = (A - B) + t_mid v + μ n.
But A - B = (x0 - x0, y0 - (-y0)) = (0, 2y0). So A-B = (0, 2y0).

Thus O-B = (0, 2y0) + t_mid v + μ n.

Similarly, O-E = (A + t_mid v + μ n) - (A + t_E v) = (t_mid - t_E) v + μ n.

Now, v = (dx, dy), n = (dy, -dx) (since n is perpendicular to v, but note that |v|^2 = dx^2+dy^2, and n has same length? Actually, if we take n = (dy, -dx), then |n|^2 = dy^2+dx^2 = |v|^2, so n is not unit; it has length |v|. But that's fine; we can incorporate that.

We have:

|O-B|^2 = |(0,2y0) + t_mid v + μ n|^2 = |(0,2y0) + t_mid v|^2 + μ^2 |n|^2 + 2μ ( (0,2y0)+ t_mid v )· n.
|O-E|^2 = |(t_mid - t_E) v + μ n|^2 = (t_mid - t_E)^2 |v|^2 + μ^2 |v|^2 + 2μ (t_mid - t_E) v·n. But v·n = dx*dy + dy*(-dx)=0. So |O-E|^2 = |v|^2 ((t_mid - t_E)^2 + μ^2).

Set them equal:

|(0,2y0) + t_mid v|^2 + μ^2 |v|^2 + 2μ ( (0,2y0)+ t_mid v )· n = |v|^2 ((t_mid - t_E)^2 + μ^2).

Cancel μ^2 |v|^2 both sides:

|(0,2y0) + t_mid v|^2 + 2μ ( (0,2y0)+ t_mid v )· n = |v|^2 (t_mid - t_E)^2.

Thus we can solve for μ:

2μ ( (0,2y0)+ t_mid v )· n = |v|^2 (t_mid - t_E)^2 - |(0,2y0) + t_mid v|^2.

Now, compute the left side dot product:

((0,2y0) + t_mid v)· n = (0,2y0)· n + t_mid v· n. But v·n=0, so it's just (0,2y0)· n = 2y0 n_y. Since n = (dy, -dx), n_y = -dx. So (0,2y0)· n = 2y0 * (-dx) = -2y0 dx. Thus left side = 2μ (-2y0 dx) = -4μ y0 dx.

So we have:

-4μ y0 dx = |v|^2 (t_mid - t_E)^2 - |(0,2y0) + t_mid v|^2.

Thus μ = - [ |v|^2 (t_mid - t_E)^2 - |(0,2y0) + t_mid v|^2 ] / (4 y0 dx).

Now, note that t_mid - t_E = (t_E+t_F)/2 - t_E = (t_F - t_E)/2. So (t_mid - t_E)^2 = (t_F - t_E)^2/4.

Also, we can compute |(0,2y0) + t_mid v|^2 = 4y0^2 + t_mid^2 |v|^2 + 4y0 t_mid dy? Because (0,2y0) dot v = 2y0 dy? Actually, (0,2y0)·v = 2y0 dy. So:

|(0,2y0) + t_mid v|^2 = 4y0^2 + t_mid^2 |v|^2 + 4y0 t_mid dy.

Now, also we have that t_E t_F =? We might need to simplify.

But maybe there is a simpler way: Use the fact that O is the circumcenter of BEF, and we can also find O as the point that is the intersection of perpendicular bisectors. Perhaps we can compute O directly using the formula for circumcenter in coordinates. But the expression for μ will involve t_mid, t_E, t_F, and the dot products. However, we can also compute the distance from O to line ℓ and radius. Perhaps we can show that the line ℓ is the polar of point B with respect to circle (AEF)? Not.

Given the time, I'll attempt to produce a synthetic proof using properties of circles and orthocenters.

y_P - y0 = -p(x0+r)/y0 - y0 = -(p(x0+r)+y0^2)/y0 = -(p(x0+r)+r^2-x0^2)/y0 = -( (x0+r)(p+r-x0) )/y0? Actually, p(x0+r)+r^2-x0^2 = p(x0+r)+(r-x0)(r+x0) = (x0+r)(p+r-x0). So y_P - y0 = -(x0+r)(p+r-x0)/y0.

And p-x0 =? p = (d+R-r)/2, x0 = (d^2 - R^2 + r^2)/(2d). So p-x0 is something.

Maybe we can find that the line AP is the reflection of the line through the orthocenter? Not.

Another observation: In the numeric example, we had t_F = 1, so F = P. That is a special case. In general, F is not P, but P is on line EF. So P lies on the line through A and the other two points. Alternatively, consider the inversion with center A that swaps the circles Ω and Γ. Let's try inversion about A with radius such that the circles become lines. Let inversion with center A and arbitrary radius r0. Then Ω becomes a line ℓ1 (since it passes through A). Maybe we can choose inversion radius such that the image of P is the orthocenter of something. Let's explore.

Set inversion about A with arbitrary radius. Then:

- Ω becomes line ℓ1 (since A on Ω). Similarly, Γ becomes line ℓ2.

Given the complexity, I think the coordinate proof is the way to go, but we need to present it clearly, with careful algebraic simplifications. Since the problem is from an olympiad, a coordinate solution is acceptable if done rigorously. Let's attempt to produce a clean coordinate proof.

We set up coordinates:

Let M = (0,0), N = (d,0) with d > 0. Let Ω: x^2 + y^2 = r^2, Γ: (x-d)^2 + y^2 = R^2, with r < R and |R-r| < d < R+r. The circles intersect at A and B. By symmetry, we can choose A = (x0, y0) with y0 > 0, B = (x0, -y0), where x0 = (d^2 - R^2 + r^2)/(2d), y0 = sqrt(r^2 - x0^2) > 0.

Now, the line MN is the x-axis. It meets Ω at C = (-r,0) and Γ at D = (d+R,0), with order C, M, N, D. So C = (-r,0), D = (d+R,0).

Now, define P as circumcenter of triangle ACD. Since C and D lie on x-axis, the perpendicular bisector of CD is vertical line x = (C_x + D_x)/2 = ( -r + d+R )/2 = (d+R-r)/2 = p. So P has x-coordinate p. Let P = (p, y_P). Since PA = PC, we have (x0-p)^2 + (y0-y_P)^2 = (-r-p)^2 + y_P^2. Expanding gives -2x0p + x0^2 + y0^2 -2y0y_P + y_P^2 = p^2 + 2pr + r^2 + y_P^2. Cancel y_P^2 and use x0^2+y0^2 = r^2. Then we get -2x0p + r^2 -2y0y_P = p^2 + 2pr + r^2 => -2x0p -2y0y_P = p^2 + 2pr => 2y0y_P = -p^2 -2pr -2x0p = -p(p+2r+2x0). So y_P = -p(p+2r+2x0)/(2y0). But we earlier found y_P = -p(x0+r)/y0. Let's check consistency: p(p+2r+2x0)/(2y0) = p(p+2r+2x0)/(2y0). Is that equal to p(x0+r)/y0? That would require p+2r+2x0 = 2(x0+r) => p+2r+2x0 = 2x0+2r => p=0, which is not. So there is a discrepancy. Let's re-derive carefully.

We have C = (-r, 0), D = (d+R, 0). Their midpoint: x = (-r + d+R)/2 = p. So perpendicular bisector of CD: x = p. So P = (p, y_P). Now, condition PA = PC: distance from P to A equals distance from P to C.

PA^2 = (x0-p)^2 + (y0-y_P)^2.
PC^2 = (-r-p)^2 + (0-y_P)^2 = (p+r)^2 + y_P^2. (since -r-p = -(p+r), square = (p+r)^2).

Set equal:

(x0-p)^2 + y0^2 - 2y0 y_P + y_P^2 = (p+r)^2 + y_P^2.

Cancel y_P^2:

(x0-p)^2 + y0^2 - 2y0 y_P = (p+r)^2.

Now, (x0-p)^2 = x0^2 - 2x0p + p^2. So:

x0^2 - 2x0p + p^2 + y0^2 - 2y0 y_P = p^2 + 2pr + r^2.

Cancel p^2:

x0^2 + y0^2 - 2x0p - 2y0 y_P = 2pr + r^2.

But x0^2+y0^2 = r^2. So:

r^2 - 2x0p - 2y0 y_P = 2pr + r^2 => -2x0p - 2y0 y_P = 2pr => Divide 2: -x0p - y0 y_P = pr => Thus y0 y_P = -pr - x0p = -p(r+x0). So y_P = -p(r+x0)/y0.

That matches our earlier expression. Good. So we have y_P = -p (r+x0)/y0.

Now, note that r+x0 > 0 because x0 ≥ -r, and if x0 = -r then A is at (-r,y0) with y0=0? Actually if x0=-r, then y0^2 = r^2 - r^2 =0, so A = (-r,0)=C, which would be degenerate. So x0 > -r, so r+x0 >0. So y_P <0.

Now, line AP: through A(x0,y0) and P(p, y_P). Its direction vector v = (p-x0, y_P-y0). We'll keep it.

Now, let's find E and F as second intersections of line AP with Ω and Γ.

Parameterize line AP: X(t) = A + t v, t real. For t=0: A; t=1: P. For Ω: |X(t)|^2 = r^2. Since |A|^2 = r^2, we have |A + t v|^2 = r^2 + 2t (A·v) + t^2 |v|^2 = r^2 => 2t (A·v) + t^2 |v|^2 = 0 => t(2A·v + t|v|^2)=0. Non-zero root: t_E = -2(A·v)/|v|^2. Compute A·v = x0(p-x0) + y0(y_P-y0). But y0 y_P = -p(r+x0). So y0 y_P - y0^2 = -p(r+x0) - y0^2. So A·v = x0(p-x0) - p(r+x0) - y0^2 = x0p - x0^2 - pr - px0 - y0^2 = -x0^2 - pr - y0^2 = -(x0^2+y0^2) - pr = -r^2 - pr = -r(p+r). So A·v = -r(p+r). Thus t_E = -2(-r(p+r))/|v|^2 = 2r(p+r)/|v|^2.

Similarly, for Γ: (X(t)-N)^2 = R^2. N=(d,0). So (A+N? Actually, (A - N + t v)^2 = R^2. Since |A-N|^2 = R^2, we get 2t ( (A-N)·v ) + t^2 |v|^2 = 0 => t_F = -2((A-N)·v)/|v|^2. Compute (A-N)·v = (x0-d)(p-x0) + y0(y_P-y0). Using the same expansion as before: (x0-d)(p-x0) = (x0-d)p - (x0-d)x0 = p x0 - p d - x0^2 + d x0. Then add y0(y_P-y0) = y0 y_P - y0^2 = -p(r+x0) - y0^2. So sum = p x0 - p d - x0^2 + d x0 - p r - p x0 - y0^2 = - p d - x0^2 + d x0 - p r - y0^2 = - p d + d x0 - p r - (x0^2+y0^2) = -p d + d x0 - p r - r^2 = d(x0-p) - r(p+r). But x0-p = -(p-x0). So (A-N)·v = -d(p-x0) - r(p+r). Thus t_F = -2(-d(p-x0)-r(p+r))/|v|^2 = 2(d(p-x0)+r(p+r))/|v|^2.

So t_E and t_F as given.

Now, define H, orthocenter of triangle PMN. M=(0,0), N=(d,0), P=(p, y_P). Since M and N are on x-axis, the altitude from P is vertical line x=p. So H lies on x=p. Let H = (p, y_H). The altitude from M is perpendicular to PN. PN vector: N-P = (d-p, -y_P). So altitude from M: through M, direction perpendicular to PN, i.e., vector (y_P, d-p) (since dot with PN: (d-p)*y_P + (-y_P)*(d-p)=0). So line: (x,y)= (0,0)+s(y_P, d-p). This line meets x=p at s = p/y_P? Actually, x = s y_P = p => s = p/y_P. Then y = s(d-p) = p(d-p)/y_P. So y_H = p(d-p)/y_P. Since y_P < 0, y_H < 0. So H = (p, p(d-p)/y_P).

We can also compute y_H using that y_P = -p(r+x0)/y0. So y_H = p(d-p) / ( -p(r+x0)/y0 ) = - (d-p) y0/(r+x0). That matches earlier expression: y_H = - (d-p) y0/(x0+r). Good.

Now, we need to prove that line ℓ through H parallel to AP (direction v) is tangent to circumcircle of triangle BEF.

Let’s compute the circle (BEF). We'll find its circumcenter O and radius R. Because the line ℓ is parallel to v, we can consider the condition that the distance from O to ℓ equals R. But maybe we can find a relationship: The point A is the midpoint of the arc EF of the circle (BEF)? Not.

Another idea: Use the fact that the reflection of H over the midpoint of MN is the circumcenter of triangle PMN? Not.

Given the heavy algebra, but the fact that we have explicit expressions for E and F, we can compute O. Since E and F are on line L: A + t v, and B is symmetric to A across x-axis, we can perhaps find that the circumcenter O of BEF is the point that is the image of P under some transformation. Let's try to compute O using the perpendicular bisector method we started earlier. We have:

Let M_EF = A + t_mid v, where t_mid = (t_E+t_F)/2. Then O = A + t_mid v + μ n, where n = (dy, -dx) is perpendicular to v (since v=(dx,dy)). But note that n is not unit; its length = |v|.

Now, we also have OB = OE. Using our earlier derivation, we found μ = - [ |v|^2 (t_mid - t_E)^2 - |(0,2y0) + t_mid v|^2 ] / (4 y0 dx). But we can simplify using t_mid - t_E = (t_F - t_E)/2.

We also have that |(0,2y0) + t_mid v|^2 = 4y0^2 + t_mid^2 |v|^2 + 4y0 t_mid dy.

Now, we can compute |v|^2 (t_mid - t_E)^2 = |v|^2 ((t_F - t_E)^2/4) = (|v|^2/4)(t_F - t_E)^2.

Thus the numerator becomes (|v|^2/4)(t_F - t_E)^2 - [4y0^2 + t_mid^2 |v|^2 + 4y0 t_mid dy].

Now, note that t_F - t_E = 2(d(p-x0)+r(p+r))/L - 2r(p+r)/L = 2(d(p-x0))/L. So t_F - t_E = 2d(p-x0)/L. And L = |v|^2.

Thus t_F - t_E = 2d(p-x0)/L.

Then (t_F - t_E)^2 = 4d^2(p-x0)^2/L^2.

So (|v|^2/4)(t_F - t_E)^2 = (L/4)* (4d^2(p-x0)^2/L^2) = d^2(p-x0)^2/L.

Thus numerator = d^2(p-x0)^2/L - [4y0^2 + t_mid^2 L + 4y0 t_mid dy].

Now, t_mid = (t_E+t_F)/2 = [2r(p+r)/L + 2(d(p-x0)+r(p+r))/L] / 2 = [2r(p+r)+2(d(p-x0)+r(p+r))/L / 2 = [2r(p+r)+2(d(p-x0)+r(p+r))]/(2L) = [2r(p+r)+2d(p-x0)+2r(p+r)]/(2L) = [2d(p-x0)+4r(p+r)]/(2L) = [d(p-x0)+2r(p+r)]/L.

Thus t_mid = (d(p-x0)+2r(p+r))/L.

Also, t_mid^2 L = (d(p-x0)+2r(p+r))^2 / L.

Now, also we need dy = y_P - y0. And we have y_P = -p(r+x0)/y0.

Thus dy = -p(r+x0)/y0 - y0 = -(p(r+x0)+y0^2)/y0 = -(p(r+x0)+(r^2-x0^2))/y0 = -( (x0+r)(p+r-x0) )/y0? Actually, p(r+x0)+r^2-x0^2 = p(r+x0)+(r-x0)(r+x0) = (r+x0)(p+r-x0). So dy = -(r+x0)(p+r-x0)/y0.

Also, p-x0 =? We have p = (d+R-r)/2, x0 = (d^2 - R^2 + r^2)/(2d). So p-x0 = (d+R-r)/2 - (d^2 - R^2 + r^2)/(2d) = [d(d+R-r) - (d^2 - R^2 + r^2)]/(2d) = [d^2 + dR - dr - d^2 + R^2 - r^2]/(2d) = [dR - dr + R^2 - r^2]/(2d) = [R(d+R) - r(d+R)]/(2d) = [(R-r)(d+R)]/(2d). So p-x0 = (R-r)(d+R)/(2d). That is a nice expression.

Also, r+x0 = r + (d^2 - R^2 + r^2)/(2d) = (2dr + d^2 - R^2 + r^2)/(2d) = (d^2 + 2dr + r^2 - R^2)/(2d) = ((d+r)^2 - R^2)/(2d) = ((d+r-R)(d+r+R))/(2d). Since circles intersect, d+r > R, so positive.

And we have y0^2 = r^2 - x0^2. But we might also compute y0^2 in terms of d,r,R. Using x0 = (d^2 - R^2 + r^2)/(2d), then y0^2 = r^2 - x0^2 = r^2 - ((d^2 - R^2 + r^2)^2/(4d^2)). This is messy but maybe we can avoid explicit y0.

Now, we have L = |v|^2 = (p-x0)^2 + dy^2.

Compute p-x0 = (R-r)(d+R)/(2d). dy = -(r+x0)(p+r-x0)/y0. But we can compute dy in terms of knowns: r+x0 = ((d+r)^2 - R^2)/(2d) = ((d+r-R)(d+r+R))/(2d). p+r-x0 = p+r-x0 = (d+R-r)/2 + r - x0 = (d+R+r)/2 - x0. And x0 = (d^2 - R^2 + r^2)/(2d). So p+r-x0 = (d+R+r)/2 - (d^2 - R^2 + r^2)/(2d) = [d(d+R+r) - (d^2 - R^2 + r^2)]/(2d) = [d^2 + dR + dr - d^2 + R^2 - r^2]/(2d) = [dR + dr + R^2 - r^2]/(2d) = [R(d+R) + r(d-R)]/(2d)? Actually, dR + dr + R^2 - r^2 = R(d+R) + r(d-R). That is not obviously factorable.

But we also have that y0^2 = r^2 - x0^2. This might simplify the expression for dy^2. Perhaps we can find a simpler expression for L using the fact that v = (p-x0, y_P-y0) and we have p = (d+R-r)/2, x0 = (d^2 - R^2 + r^2)/(2d), and y_P = -p(r+x0)/y0. So v = (p-x0, -p(r+x0)/y0 - y0). Given the difficulty, I think we should produce a coordinate proof that is clear and rigorous, even if long. Since the problem is likely from an IMO Shortlist, a coordinate solution is acceptable. We'll present the proof with careful algebraic simplifications, using the MN as x-axis system. We'll compute O_BEF and then the tangency condition using vector algebra. We'll try to simplify the algebra by using the expressions we have.

Let's attempt to compute O directly using the fact that B, E, F are three points. We can compute the circumcenter using the formula:

For points X, Y, Z, the circumcenter is given by (|X|^2 (Y - Z) + |Y|^2 (Z - X) + |Z|^2 (X - Y)) / (2 (X × Y + Y × Z + Z × X)? Actually, there is a formula using determinants. But maybe we can find that O is the point that satisfies:

Another approach: Use coordinates but with a more clever parameterization. Let’s set up coordinates with A at origin, and let the line AP be the x-axis. Then we have M = (α,β), N = (γ,δ). The circles: x^2+y^2 = 2α x + 2β y and x^2+y^2 = 2γ x + 2δ y. The points E and F are on x-axis: E=(2α,0), F=(2γ,0). The point B is the other intersection of the circles. We can compute B coordinates in terms of α,β,γ,δ. Then we need to compute H, orthocenter of triangle PMN, with P=(p,0) on x-axis. And then show that the horizontal line through H is tangent to circle through B, E, F.

Now, we have the relation that P is circumcenter of triangle ACD. In this coordinate system, A=0, P=(p,0). And we have C and D are points on line MN (centers) and on circles. We can compute C and D in terms of M,N. The condition that P is circumcenter gives:

p C_x = |C|^2/2, p D_x = |D|^2/2.

Thus we have two equations that determine p and also give a relation between M,N. So we can solve for p.

Now, we can also compute H: orthocenter of triangle PMN. Since M=(α,β), N=(γ,δ), P=(p,0). The orthocenter H can be computed using the formula: H = M + N + P - 2 O, where O is circumcenter of triangle PMN. But we can also compute H directly as intersection of altitudes. Since P is on x-axis, altitude from P is perpendicular to MN. So H lies on line through P perpendicular to MN. That line is given by: (x,y) = (p,0) + u (δ-β, -(γ-α))? Actually, direction vector perpendicular to MN = (γ-α, δ-β) is (δ-β, -(γ-α)). So line: (p + u(δ-β), -u(γ-α)). So H = (p + u_H (δ-β), -u_H (γ-α)). Now, also H must lie on altitude from M, which is perpendicular to PN. PN vector: N-P = (γ-p, δ). So altitude from M: through M, direction perpendicular to PN: (δ, -(γ-p)). So line: (α,β) + s (δ, -(γ-p)). The intersection gives equations.

Alternatively, we can compute y_H = -u_H (γ-α). So we need u_H.

Now, we also have B = (x_B, y_B) from earlier: B = t (v, -u) with u = α-γ, v = β-δ, t = 2(α v - β u)/L^2, where L^2 = u^2+v^2.

Now, the circle (BEF): E=(2α,0), F=(2γ,0), B=(x_B,y_B). Its circumcenter O = (α+γ, o_y) because perpendicular bisector of EF is vertical x = α+γ. So O_x = α+γ. And radius R = distance from O to E = sqrt((α+γ-2α)^2 + o_y^2) = sqrt((γ-α)^2 + o_y^2). Also, OB = R gives:

(α+γ - x_B)^2 + (o_y - y_B)^2 = (γ-α)^2 + o_y^2.

Simplify:

(α+γ - x_B)^2 + o_y^2 - 2 o_y y_B + y_B^2 = (γ-α)^2 + o_y^2

=> (α+γ - x_B)^2 - 2 o_y y_B + y_B^2 = (γ-α)^2

=> 2 o_y y_B = (α+γ - x_B)^2 + y_B^2 - (γ-α)^2

=> o_y = [ (α+γ - x_B)^2 + y_B^2 - (γ-α)^2 ] / (2 y_B), assuming y_B ≠ 0.

Now, the horizontal line through H is y = y_H. For it to be tangent to circle (BEF), we need |y_H - o_y| = R = sqrt((γ-α)^2 + o_y^2). This is equivalent to (y_H - o_y)^2 = (γ-α)^2 + o_y^2 => y_H^2 - 2 y_H o_y + o_y^2 = (γ-α)^2 + o_y^2 => y_H^2 - 2 y_H o_y = (γ-α)^2 => 2 y_H o_y = y_H^2 - (γ-α)^2 => o_y = (y_H^2 - (γ-α)^2)/(2 y_H). So we need to verify that the expression for o_y from B satisfies this.

Thus we need to compute y_H and then check that the o_y computed from B satisfies that relation. So we need to compute p and y_H in terms of α,β,γ,δ. Then compute B in terms of α,β,γ,δ. Then compute o_y and then show that o_y = (y_H^2 - (γ-α)^2)/(2 y_H). That is an algebraic identity.

Let's attempt to derive p from the condition that P is circumcenter of ACD.

In the A=0, AP=x-axis system, we have A=0, P=(p,0). The points C and D are on line MN (through M and N). Let's compute M and N coordinates. We have:

M = (α,β)
N = (γ,δ)

Define d = N-M = (γ-α, δ-β). L = |d|.

Now, C = M - (r/L) d, where r = |M| = sqrt(α^2+β^2).
D = M + (1+R/L) d, where R = |N| = sqrt(γ^2+δ^2).

Now, condition P is circumcenter of ACD. Since A=0, we have:

P·C = |C|^2/2  => p C_x = |C|^2/2
P·D = |D|^2/2  => p D_x = |D|^2/2

Thus we have:

C_x = |C|^2/(2p)
D_x = |D|^2/(2p)

Now, compute C_x and |C|^2. As before:

|C|^2 = 2r^2 - (2r/L) M·d, and M·d = (R^2 - r^2 - L^2)/2.
So |C|^2 = 2r^2 - (2r/L)*((R^2 - r^2 - L^2)/2) = 2r^2 - (r/L)(R^2 - r^2 - L^2) = 2r^2 - (rR^2)/L + (r^3)/L + rL.

Also, C_x = α - (r/L)(γ-α) = α - (r/L)(γ-α).

Thus p = |C|^2/(2C_x). So p = [2r^2 - (rR^2)/L + (r^3)/L + rL] / [2(α - (r/L)(γ-α))].

This is still messy. But perhaps we can find a simpler expression for p by using the fact that P is also the circumcenter of triangle ACD, so it is the center of the circle through A, C, D. In complex numbers, if A=0, then the circumcenter is given by (c d (c̄ - d̄))/(c d̄ - c̄ d). So p = (c d (c̄ - d̄))/(c d̄ - c̄ d). Since p is real (on x-axis), this gives a condition.

But maybe we can avoid explicit p by using the fact that the line through H parallel to AP is tangent to (BEF). This might be equivalent to some cross ratio being harmonic, or that the point H is the midpoint of the segment connecting the centers of the circles? Not.

Given the time, I'll try to produce a proof using coordinate geometry but with a more systematic approach: Compute everything in terms of the original parameters (x0,y0,r,R,d) and then verify the tangency condition using algebraic manipulation, perhaps with the help of computer algebra. Since we are to produce a solution, we need to present the algebra in a clear way. Let's try to simplify the expressions for E and F and then compute O_BEF.

We have:

M = (0,0), N = (d,0)

Define v = (dx, dy) = (p-x0, y_P-y0)

We have t_E = 2r(p+r)/L, t_F = 2(d(p-x0)+r(p+r))/L, where L = dx^2+dy^2.

Now, E = A + t_E v, F = A + t_F v.

We want to find the circumcenter O of triangle BEF. Since B = (x0, -y0), E = (x0 + t_E dx, y0 + t_E dy), F = (x0 + t_F dx, y0 + t_F dy). Let’s denote:

Let’s translate coordinates to shift A to origin: define new coordinates:

A' = (0,0)
B' = B - A = (0, -2y0)
E' = E - A = (t_E dx, t_E dy)
F' = F - A = (t_F dx, t_F dy)

Now, we have points: A'=0, B'=(0, -2y0), E'=(t_E dx, t_E dy), F'=(t_F dx, t_F dy). This is similar to the A=0 system we had earlier, but with B' on y-axis, and the line AP becomes the line through origin with direction v. So we have A=0, B=(0, -2y0), and E,F on line through origin with direction v. That is a simpler configuration: The circumcircle of triangle BEF passes through B and two points on a line through A (origin). So we can find its circumcenter.

Let’s denote:

Let a = t_E, b = t_F. Then E = (a dx, a dy), F = (b dx, b dy). And B = (0, -2y0).

Now, the circumcenter O' of triangle BEF. Since E and F lie on line through origin with direction v, the perpendicular bisector of EF is the line through the midpoint of EF, perpendicular to v. That midpoint is M_EF = ((a+b)/2 dx, (a+b)/2 dy) = t_mid v, where t_mid = (a+b)/2. The direction perpendicular to v is n = (dy, -dx). So O' = t_mid v + μ n for some μ.

Now, O' must satisfy that distance to B equals distance to E (or F). So |O' - B|^2 = |O' - E|^2.

Compute O' - B = t_mid v + μ n - (0, -2y0) = t_mid v + μ n + (0, 2y0) (since minus B gives + (0, 2y0)? Actually, B = (0, -2y0), so O' - B = (t_mid dx + μ dy, t_mid dy - μ dx) - (0, -2y0) = (t_mid dx + μ dy, t_mid dy - μ dx + 2y0). And O' - E = (t_mid v + μ n) - (a v) = (t_mid - a) v + μ n.

Now, as before, v·n=0. So |O'-E|^2 = (t_mid - a)^2 |v|^2 + μ^2 |v|^2 = |v|^2 ((t_mid - a)^2 + μ^2).

And |O'-B|^2 = |(t_mid dx + μ dy, t_mid dy - μ dx + 2y0)|^2 = (t_mid dx + μ dy)^2 + (t_mid dy - μ dx + 2y0)^2.

Expand:

= t_mid^2 dx^2 + 2 t_mid dx μ dy + μ^2 dy^2 + (t_mid dy - μ dx)^2 + 4y0(t_mid dy - μ dx) + 4y0^2

= t_mid^2 dx^2 + 2 t_mid dx μ dy + μ^2 dy^2 + t_mid^2 dy^2 - 2 t_mid dy μ dx + μ^2 dx^2 + 4y0(t_mid dy - μ dx) + 4y0^2

= t_mid^2 (dx^2+dy^2) + μ^2 (dx^2+dy^2) + 4y0(t_mid dy - μ dx) + 4y0^2

= |v|^2 (t_mid^2 + μ^2) + 4y0(t_mid dy - μ dx) + 4y0^2.

Set equal to |v|^2 ((t_mid - a)^2 + μ^2). Cancel μ^2 |v|^2:

|v|^2 t_mid^2 + 4y0(t_mid dy - μ dx) + 4y0^2 = |v|^2 (t_mid - a)^2.

Thus:

4y0(t_mid dy - μ dx) = |v|^2 ((t_mid - a)^2 - t_mid^2) - 4y0^2.

Compute ((t_mid - a)^2 - t_mid^2) = (t_mid^2 - 2t_mid a + a^2) - t_mid^2 = -2t_mid a + a^2 = a(a - 2t_mid). But note that a = t_E, and t_mid = (a+b)/2, so a - 2t_mid = a - (a+b) = -b. So ((t_mid - a)^2 - t_mid^2) = a(-b) = -a b.

Thus:

4y0(t_mid dy - μ dx) = |v|^2 (-a b) - 4y0^2 = -ab |v|^2 - 4y0^2.

So:

4y0(t_mid dy - μ dx) = -ab |v|^2 - 4y0^2.

Thus:

t_mid dy - μ dx = - (ab |v|^2 + 4y0^2)/(4y0).

Now, we can solve for μ:

μ dx = t_mid dy + (ab |v|^2 + 4y0^2)/(4y0).

Thus μ = [ t_mid dy + (ab |v|^2 + 4y0^2)/(4y0) ] / dx, provided dx ≠ 0. In our numeric, dx>0.

Now, we have O' = t_mid v + μ n.

Thus O' = (t_mid dx + μ dy, t_mid dy - μ dx).

Now, we need to show that the line ℓ through H parallel to v is tangent to circle (BEF). In the translated system, H corresponds to H' = H - A = (p - x0, y_H - y0) = (dx, y_H - y0). And ℓ is line through H' with direction v. So ℓ: X = H' + s v.

Now, the circle (BEF) in translated system is centered at O' and radius R = |O'-B|. We need to show that distance from O' to ℓ equals R.

The distance from O' to ℓ is |(O' - H') × v| / |v|. So we need to verify:

|(O' - H') × v|^2 = |v|^2 R^2.

Now, O' - H' = (t_mid v + μ n) - H' = t_mid v + μ n - H'. But H' = (dx, y_H - y0). And we know v = (dx, dy). Also, n = (dy, -dx). So we can compute cross product.

Maybe we can find that O' lies on the line through H' perpendicular to v? That would mean that O' - H' is parallel to n. If that is true, then the foot of perpendicular from O' to ℓ is H', and distance = |O'-H'|. Then we need |O'-H'| = R. That would be a simpler condition: O' - H' is parallel to n, and |O'-H'| = R. Let's test numeric: In numeric, we had O = (1, -1.2909) in original coordinates, H = (1.5, -0.3873), A = (0.25,0.9682). Then O' = O - A = (0.75, -2.2591). H' = H - A = (1.25, -1.3555). v = (1.25, -2.9047). n = (-2.9047, -1.25). Compute O' - H' = (0.75-1.25, -2.2591+1.3555) = (-0.5, -0.9036). Is that parallel to n? n = (-2.9047, -1.25). The ratio: (-0.5)/(-2.9047)=0.172, (-0.9036)/(-1.25)=0.7229, not equal. So not parallel. So O' - H' is not parallel to n. So the foot is not H'.

Thus we need to compute the cross product directly.

Given the complexity, perhaps we can find a synthetic transformation: Consider the homothety centered at A that sends Ω to Γ. Under this homothety, the line AP is invariant, and E maps to F. Also, the circle (ACD) maps to something? Since P is the circumcenter of ACD, and A is fixed, the image of the circle (ACD) under the homothety is a circle through A and the images of C and D. The image of C is some point on Γ? Not necessarily.

Another synthetic idea: Use the fact that H is the orthocenter of triangle PMN, and the line through H parallel to AP is the reflection of the line through A and the circumcenter of triangle PMN? Not.

Given the time, I'll try to produce a coordinate proof that is rigorous but maybe not overly long by using vector algebra and simplifications. Not.

Actually, the polar of point X with respect to a circle centered at O with radius R is the line: (X-O)·(Y-O) = R^2. So polar of A: (A-O)·(Y-O) = R^2. Compute A-O = (-0.75, 2.2591). R^2 = 0.6665. Given the difficulty, I think the coordinate approach with the MN as x-axis system and using vector algebra to verify the identity is doable if we keep expressions and use the relations we have. Let's attempt to compute the condition using the fact that the line ℓ is tangent to circle (BEF) iff the equation for the intersection of ℓ and the circle has discriminant zero. That is:

| (H - O) + s v |^2 = R^2.

We can compute the discriminant in terms of the dot products. The discriminant Δ = 4[ (H-O)·v ]^2 - 4|v|^2 (|H-O|^2 - R^2) = 0 => [ (H-O)·v ]^2 = |v|^2 (|H-O|^2 - R^2).

Now, we can compute H-O and R. We have O = circumcenter of BEF. In the translated system with A=0, we have O' = t_mid v + μ n. And H' = H - A = (dx, y_H - y0). And we have B' = (0, -2y0). Also, we have expressions for t_mid, μ, etc. So we need to compute O' - H' and then the dot product with v.

Given that we have explicit formulas for t_mid and μ, we can compute (H'-O')·v = - (O'-H')·v. So we need (O'-H')·v. But O' = t_mid v + μ n, so (O'-H')·v = t_mid |v|^2 + μ (n·v) - H'·v. Since n·v=0, we get = t_mid |v|^2 - H'·v. Thus (H'-O')·v = -t_mid |v|^2 + H'·v.

Now, compute H'·v = (dx, y_H - y0)·(dx, dy) = dx^2 + (y_H - y0) dy = dx^2 + (y_H - y0) dy.

Thus (H'-O')·v = -t_mid |v|^2 + dx^2 + (y_H - y0) dy.

Now, we also need |H'-O'|^2 - R^2. R^2 = |O'-B'|^2 = |(t_mid v + μ n) - (0, -2y0)|^2 = |t_mid v + μ n + (0, 2y0)|^2. As before, |O'-B'|^2 = |v|^2 (t_mid^2+μ^2) + 4y0(t_mid dy - μ dx) + 4y0^2. But we also have that O' is the circumcenter, so OB' = OE' = etc. But we can compute |H'-O'|^2 - R^2 = |(H'-O')|^2 - |O'-B'|^2.

We can compute (H'-O') = H' - (t_mid v + μ n) = (H' - t_mid v) - μ n.

Then |H'-O'|^2 = |H' - t_mid v|^2 + μ^2 |n|^2 - 2μ (H' - t_mid v)· n.

Since n·v=0, we have H'·n =? H' = (dx, y_H - y0). n = (dy, -dx). So H'·n = dx dy + (y_H - y0)(-dx) = dx dy - dx(y_H - y0) = dx (dy - y_H + y0). Also, t_mid v·n =0. So (H' - t_mid v)· n = H'·n.

Thus |H'-O'|^2 = |H' - t_mid v|^2 + μ^2 |v|^2 - 2μ H'·n.

Now, |H' - t_mid v|^2 = |H'|^2 + t_mid^2 |v|^2 - 2 t_mid H'·v.

So |H'-O'|^2 = |H'|^2 + t_mid^2 |v|^2 - 2 t_mid H'·v + μ^2 |v|^2 - 2μ H'·n.

Then |H'-O'|^2 - R^2 = [|H'|^2 + t_mid^2 |v|^2 - 2 t_mid H'·v + μ^2 |v|^2 - 2μ H'·n] - [|v|^2 (t_mid^2+μ^2) + 4y0(t_mid dy - μ dx) + 4y0^2] = |H'|^2 - 2 t_mid H'·v - 2μ H'·n - 4y0(t_mid dy - μ dx) - 4y0^2.

Simplify: |H'|^2 = dx^2 + (y_H - y0)^2.

And H'·v = dx^2 + (y_H - y0) dy.

Thus -2 t_mid H'·v = -2 t_mid (dx^2 + (y_H - y0) dy). So the expression becomes:

|H'|^2 - 2 t_mid H'·v - 2μ H'·n - 4y0 t_mid dy + 4y0 μ dx - 4y0^2.

= |H'|^2 - 2 t_mid (dx^2 + (y_H - y0) dy) - 2μ H'·n - 4y0 t_mid dy + 4y0 μ dx - 4y0^2.

Now, |H'|^2 = dx^2 + (y_H - y0)^2. So combine with -2 t_mid dx^2: = dx^2 (1-2t_mid) + (y_H - y0)^2 - 2 t_mid (y_H - y0) dy - 2μ H'·n - 4y0 t_mid dy + 4y0 μ dx - 4y0^2.

Thus |H'-O'|^2 - R^2 = dx^2 (1-2t_mid) + (y_H - y0)^2 - 2 t_mid (y_H - y0) dy - 2μ H'·n - 4y0 t_mid dy + 4y0 μ dx - 4y0^2.

Now, we also need the left side of the tangency condition: [ (H'-O')·v ]^2 = |v|^2 (|H'-O'|^2 - R^2). But we already have (H'-O')·v = -t_mid |v|^2 + H'·v = -t_mid L + (dx^2 + (y_H - y0) dy), where L = |v|^2.

So (H'-O')·v = -t_mid L + dx^2 + (y_H - y0) dy.

Thus we need to verify:

(-t_mid L + dx^2 + (y_H - y0) dy)^2 = L ( |H'-O'|^2 - R^2 ).

That is a messy equation. However, we have explicit expressions for t_mid and μ in terms of a,b, etc. And we have a,b = t_E, t_F. So we can plug in.

Let me try to understand the configuration deeper.

We have two circles Ω and Γ intersecting at A and B. Let the line through the centers MN intersect the circles at C (on Ω) and D (on Γ), with order C-M-N-D. So C is on the ray from M away from N, D is on the ray from N away from M. Then P is circumcenter of triangle ACD. So P is the center of the circle through A, C, D. Since C and D are on the line of centers, the circle (ACD) has its center on the perpendicular bisector of CD, which is a line perpendicular to MN. So P lies on a line perpendicular to MN. In fact, the perpendicular bisector of CD is the line through the midpoint of CD perpendicular to CD. Since CD is on MN, the perpendicular bisector is perpendicular to MN. So P lies on that line.

Now, consider the homothety with center A that sends Ω to Γ. Under this homothety, the point C (on Ω) maps to some point C' on Γ. Since C is on MN, and the homothety sends line MN to a line parallel to MN, the image C' lies on a line through A parallel to MN. But D is also on Γ and on MN. So D and C' are both on Γ, but generally not the same. So the homothety does not map C to D.

Now, consider the circle (ACD) centered at P. Its radius is PA. The line AP is a chord of this circle. The points E and F are the second intersections of AP with Ω and Γ. So we have AE and AF are chords of Ω and Γ. Since PA = PC = PD, we have that P is equidistant from A, C, D. So the circle (ACD) is the circumcircle of triangle ACD, with center P.

Now, note that the triangle PMN: M and N are centers of Ω and Γ. Since P is on the perpendicular bisector of CD, which is perpendicular to MN, the line through P perpendicular to MN is the line that is the perpendicular bisector of CD. So PH is vertical. H is orthocenter of triangle PMN, so H lies on the altitude from P. So H is the foot of the altitude? No, orthocenter is intersection of altitudes. Since M,N are on x-axis, the altitude from P is vertical line x=p, so H has x=p. So H is on that vertical line.

Now, the line through H parallel to AP is ℓ. So ℓ is a line through H with the same slope as AP.

We need to show that ℓ is tangent to circle (BEF). Perhaps we can show that ℓ is the line through H that is symmetric to the line through A and the circumcenter of triangle PMN? Not.

Another synthetic idea: Use the fact that the reflection of H over the midpoint of MN is the circumcenter of triangle PMN? Since M and N are on x-axis, the reflection of H over MN is the point that is the midpoint of the arc? Not.

Actually, in any triangle, the reflection of the orthocenter over a side lies on the circumcircle. So reflect H over side MN: since MN is the x-axis, the reflection of H over MN is H_r = (p, -y_H). That point lies on the circumcircle of triangle PMN. So H_r is on the circumcircle of PMN. Also, note that the line AP: is there a relation that the circumcircle of PMN and the circle (ACD) are orthogonal? Not.

So the circle (BEF) is the reflection of the circle through A, E', F' across x-axis. But we don't know that circle.

Given the difficulty, I'll try to produce a proof using complex numbers with the A=0, AP=x-axis system. That might simplify because E and F are real. Let's do that.

Set A = 0, and let AP be the real axis. Then we have:

M = α + iβ, N = γ + iδ.
Ω: x^2+y^2 = 2α x + 2β y => |z|^2 = 2 Re(α z) + 2 Re(β z)? Actually, in complex form, if we write z = x+iy, then |z|^2 = z \bar{z}. The circle equation is z \bar{z} = 2α x + 2β y = 2 Re(α z) + 2 Re(β z)? Not exactly, because α is complex, and 2α x + 2β y = 2 Re(α z) + 2 Re(β z)? Let's see: α = α_x + iα_y, then α z = (α_x + iα_y)(x+iy) = α_x x - α_y y + i(α_x y + α_y x). So Re(α z) = α_x x - α_y y. That is not 2α x + 2β y. So the standard form for a circle through origin is |z|^2 = 2 Re( \bar{m} z ) where m is the center? Actually, if center is m, then circle equation: |z-m|^2 = |m|^2 => z\bar{z} - \bar{m}z - m\bar{z} + |m|^2 = |m|^2 => z\bar{z} = \bar{m}z + m\bar{z} = 2 Re( \bar{m} z). So the circle through origin with center m has equation z\bar{z} = \bar{m}z + m\bar{z}. So we can write: |z|^2 = 2 Re( \bar{m} z). Thus in our A=0 system, the circle Ω has center M = m, and equation |z|^2 = \bar{m}z + m\bar{z}. Similarly, Γ: |z|^2 = \bar{n}z + n\bar{z}.

Now, the points E and F are the second intersections of the real axis (since AP is real axis) with Ω and Γ. On the real axis, z = t (real). Then equation: t^2 = \bar{m} t + m t = 2 Re(m) t? Actually, if t is real, then \bar{m}t + m t = 2 Re(m) t? Because m t + \bar{m} t = (m+\bar{m})t = 2 Re(m) t. So t^2 = 2 Re(m) t => t(t - 2 Re(m)) = 0. So the nonzero root is t = 2 Re(m). So E = 2 Re(m). Similarly, F = 2 Re(n). So indeed, E = 2α (since α = Re(m) if we set m = α + iβ), and F = 2γ.

Now, the other intersection B of the circles is the second intersection of the radical axis with the circles. The radical axis is given by |z|^2 - (\bar{m}z+m\bar{z}) = |z|^2 - (\bar{n}z+n\bar{z}) => \bar{m}z+m\bar{z} = \bar{n}z+n\bar{z} => (m-n)\bar{z} + (\bar{m}-\bar{n})z = 0 => 2 Re((m-n)\bar{z}) = 0? Actually, (m-n)\bar{z} + (\bar{m}-\bar{n})z = 2 Re((m-n)\bar{z}) = 0. So the radical axis is the line through origin: Re((m-n)\bar{z})=0. This is a line through origin. So B lies on that line. So we can write B = k * i (m-n) maybe? Since if we set z = i t (m-n) for real t, then (m-n)\bar{z} = (m-n) * (-i t (\bar{m}-\bar{n}))? Not.

Better: Let u = m-n. Then the radical axis is Re(u\bar{z})=0. This is the line through origin perpendicular to u (if we consider complex numbers as vectors). Actually, the equation Re(u\bar{z})=0 is the line through origin with direction i u (since if z = i t u, then u\bar{z} = u * (-i t \bar{u}) = -i t |u|^2, real part = 0). So the radical axis is the line through origin with direction i u. So B = t * i u for some real t ≠ 0. Then B = t i (m-n). Now, we can find t by plugging into one circle equation: |B|^2 = \bar{m}B + m\bar{B}. Compute:

|B|^2 = t^2 |u|^2.
\bar{m}B + m\bar{B} = \bar{m} t i u + m t (-i) \bar{u} = t i (\bar{m} u - m \bar{u}) = t i ( \bar{m} u - m \bar{u} ). But note that \bar{m} u - m \bar{u} = \bar{m} (m-n) - m (\bar{m}-\bar{n}) = \bar{m}m - \bar{m}n - m\bar{m} + m\bar{n} = - \bar{m}n + m\bar{n} = 2i Im(m\bar{n})? Actually, m\bar{n} - \bar{m}n = 2i Im(m\bar{n}). So \bar{m} u - m \bar{u} = m\bar{n} - \bar{m}n = 2i Im(m\bar{n}). So that expression is purely imaginary. Then t i times that gives t i * (2i Im(m\bar{n})) = -2t Im(m\bar{n}). So the equation becomes t^2 |u|^2 = -2t Im(m\bar{n}). Since t ≠ 0, we have t |u|^2 = -2 Im(m\bar{n}). Thus t = -2 Im(m\bar{n}) / |u|^2.

Thus B = t i u = i u * (-2 Im(m\bar{n}) / |u|^2) = -2i u Im(m\bar{n}) / |u|^2.

We can also write B = -2i (m-n) Im(m\bar{n}) / |m-n|^2. That's a nice complex expression.

Now, we need to find H, orthocenter of triangle PMN, where P = (p,0) on real axis. In complex numbers, P = p (real). M = m, N = n. The orthocenter H can be computed as: H = m + n + p - 2 O, where O is circumcenter of triangle PMN. But we can also compute H directly: Since P is on real axis, the altitude from P is perpendicular to MN. The direction of MN is n-m. A vector perpendicular to n-m is i(n-m). So line through P: z = p + s i (n-m). The altitude from M: through M, perpendicular to PN. PN vector: n-p. Perpendicular direction: i(n-p). So line: z = m + t i (n-p). Intersection gives H. We can solve.

Alternatively, we can use the fact that in a triangle, the orthocenter is given by the sum of the vertices minus twice the circumcenter. The circumcenter of triangle with vertices p, m, n can be found using formula: O = (p(m\bar{n} - \bar{m}n) + m(n\bar{p} - \bar{n}p) + n(p\bar{m} - \bar{p}m)) / (2(p\bar{m} - \bar{p}m + m\bar{n} - \bar{m}n + n\bar{p} - \bar{n}p)? Not.

Given the complexity, maybe we can find a simpler relation: The line through H parallel to AP (real axis) is horizontal line y = y_H. So we need to show that the horizontal line through H is tangent to circle through B, E, F. That is, the circle (BEF) has a horizontal tangent at y = y_H. So the y-coordinate of H is such that the circle is tangent to that horizontal line. In complex terms, if we set H = (h_x, h_y), then the horizontal line is Im(z) = h_y. For the circle with center O = (o_x, o_y) and radius R, the horizontal tangents are Im(z) = o_y ± R. So we need h_y = o_y ± R.

Thus we need to compute o_y and R from B, E, F. And we have E = 2α, F = 2γ, B = -2i (m-n) Im(m\bar{n}) / |m-n|^2. In terms of m,n, we can compute the circumcenter O. Now, we have m = α + iβ, n = γ + iδ. Then:

u = m-n = (α-γ) + i(β-δ).
|u|^2 = (α-γ)^2 + (β-δ)^2.
Im(m\bar{n}) = Im((α+iβ)(γ-iδ)) = α(-δ) + βγ = βγ - αδ? Actually, (α+iβ)(γ-iδ) = αγ - iαδ + iβγ + βδ = (αγ+βδ) + i(βγ-αδ). So Im = βγ - αδ. So Im(m\bar{n}) = βγ - αδ.

Thus B = -2i u (βγ - αδ) / |u|^2 = -2i ( (α-γ) + i(β-δ) ) (βγ-αδ) / |u|^2 = -2i (α-γ)(βγ-αδ)/|u|^2 -2i^2 (β-δ)(βγ-αδ)/|u|^2 = -2i (α-γ)(βγ-αδ)/|u|^2 + 2 (β-δ)(βγ-αδ)/|u|^2.

So x_B = 2 (β-δ)(βγ-αδ)/|u|^2, and y_B = -2 (α-γ)(βγ-αδ)/|u|^2.

Now, note that E = 2α, F = 2γ. So the vertical line x = α+γ.

Now, we need to compute o_y = [ (α+γ - x_B)^2 + y_B^2 - (γ-α)^2 ] / (2 y_B). That will be an expression in α,β,γ,δ.

Now, we also need y_H, the y-coordinate of H. We need to compute H from the condition that P is circumcenter of triangle ACD. In this system, A=0, P=p real. And we have M,N. The condition that P is circumcenter of triangle ACD gives equations:

P·C = |C|^2/2, P·D = |D|^2/2.

We can compute C and D in terms of m,n. Since we have complex numbers, we can write:

C = m - (|m|/|u|) u, D = m + (1+|n|/|u|) u.

In complex form, C = m - (|m|/|u|) (n-m) = m - (|m|/|u|) n + (|m|/|u|) m = m(1+|m|/|u|) - (|m|/|u|) n.
Similarly, D = m + (1+|n|/|u|) (n-m) = m + (1+|n|/|u|) n - (1+|n|/|u|) m = (|n|/|u|) m + (1+|n|/|u|) n? Wait, compute: m + (1+|n|/|u|)(n-m) = m + (1+|n|/|u|)n - (1+|n|/|u|)m = m(1 - (1+|n|/|u|)) + (1+|n|/|u|)n = m(-|n|/|u|) + (1+|n|/|u|)n. So D = (1+|n|/|u|) n - (|n|/|u|) m.

Thus C = m(1+|m|/|u|) - (|m|/|u|) n.
D = n(1+|n|/|u|) - (|n|/|u|) m.

Now, the condition P is circumcenter of triangle with vertices 0, C, D. In complex numbers, the circumcenter of triangle with vertices 0, c, d is given by (c d (c̄ - d̄))/(c d̄ - c̄ d). So we have:

p = (c d (c̄ - d̄))/(c d̄ - c̄ d). Since p is real, we get a relation.

We can compute c d. Let's denote r = |m|, R = |n|, L = |u|. Then:

c = m(1+r/L) - (r/L) n.
d = n(1+R/L) - (R/L) m.

We can compute c d. This might be messy, but perhaps we can find p in terms of m,n.

Given the time, I think we need to accept that a synthetic solution exists but we cannot produce it in the time. However, we are to produce a rigorous proof. Since the problem is from an olympiad, a coordinate solution is acceptable. I'll present a coordinate solution in the MN as x-axis system, but I'll try to simplify the algebra by using vector identities and the relations we have. I'll present the proof step by step, with careful justification.

I'll define:

Let M = (0,0), N = (d,0) with d > 0. Let r = |M A|? Actually, we have Ω: x^2+y^2 = r^2, Γ: (x-d)^2+y^2 = R^2, with r < R and |R-r| < d < R+r. Let A = (x0, y0) with y0 > 0, B = (x0, -y0). Then x0 = (d^2 - R^2 + r^2)/(2d), y0 = sqrt(r^2 - x0^2) > 0.

Define p = (d+R-r)/2, so that C = (-r,0), D = (d+R,0) and the perpendicular bisector of CD is x = p.

Now, P is the circumcenter of triangle ACD. Since C and D lie on x-axis, the perpendicular bisector of CD is x = p, so P has x-coordinate p. Let P = (p, y_P). From PA = PC, we have (x0-p)^2 + (y0-y_P)^2 = (p+r)^2 + y_P^2. Expanding gives (x0-p)^2 + y0^2 - 2y0y_P + y_P^2 = p^2+2pr+r^2+y_P^2. Using x0^2+y0^2 = r^2, we get -2x0p + r^2 -2y0y_P = 2pr + r^2 => -2x0p -2y0y_P = 2pr => y0y_P = -p(x0+r) => y_P = -p(x0+r)/y0.

Thus P = (p, -p(x0+r)/y0).

Now, line AP: direction v = (p-x0, y_P - y0). We'll denote v = (dx, dy). Then t_E = 2r(p+r)/|v|^2, t_F = 2(d(p-x0)+r(p+r))/|v|^2. So E = A + t_E v, F = A + t_F v.

Now, H is orthocenter of triangle PMN. M=(0,0), N=(d,0), P=(p, y_P). The altitude from P is vertical line x=p. The altitude from M is perpendicular to PN. PN vector = (d-p, -y_P). A direction vector perpendicular to PN is (y_P, d-p). So altitude from M: parametric (0,0)+s(y_P, d-p). Setting x = p gives s = p/y_P. Then y = s(d-p) = p(d-p)/y_P. Thus H = (p, p(d-p)/y_P). Since y_P < 0, we have H_y = p(d-p)/y_P < 0. So H = (p, h_y) with h_y = p(d-p)/y_P.

Now, we need to prove that the line ℓ through H parallel to v is tangent to the circumcircle of triangle BEF.

We will compute the circumcircle of BEF. Let’s denote:

B = (x0, -y0)
E = (x0 + t_E dx, y0 + t_E dy)
F = (x0 + t_F dx, y0 + t_F dy)

We'll find the circumcenter O of triangle BEF. Since E and F lie on the line L through A with direction v, and B is the reflection of A across the x-axis, we can use the following approach: Consider the translation that sends A to the origin. Define new coordinates:

Let X' = X - A. Then:

A' = (0,0)
B' = (0, -2y0)
E' = (t_E dx, t_E dy)
F' = (t_F dx, t_F dy)

Now, we need to find the circumcenter O' of triangle B'E'F'. Since B' is on the y-axis, and E',F' are on the line through origin with direction v, the perpendicular bisector of E'F' is the line through the midpoint of E'F' perpendicular to v. Let’s denote:

t_mid = (t_E + t_F)/2.
M_EF = t_mid v = (t_mid dx, t_mid dy).
Let n = (dy, -dx), which is perpendicular to v. Then O' = t_mid v + μ n for some μ.

Now, O' must satisfy that |O' - B'|^2 = |O' - E'|^2. We can solve for μ.

Compute:

|O' - E'|^2 = | (t_mid - t_E) v + μ n |^2 = (t_mid - t_E)^2 |v|^2 + μ^2 |v|^2 (since v·n=0). So = |v|^2 ((t_mid - t_E)^2 + μ^2).

Now, |O' - B'|^2 = | t_mid v + μ n - (0, -2y0) |^2 = | t_mid v + μ n + (0, 2y0) |^2 = (t_mid dx + μ dy)^2 + (t_mid dy - μ dx + 2y0)^2.

Expand:

= t_mid^2 dx^2 + 2 t_mid dx μ dy + μ^2 dy^2 + (t_mid dy - μ dx)^2 + 4y0(t_mid dy - μ dx) + 4y0^2

= t_mid^2 (dx^2+dy^2) + μ^2 (dx^2+dy^2) + 4y0(t_mid dy - μ dx) + 4y0^2

= |v|^2 (t_mid^2 + μ^2) + 4y0(t_mid dy - μ dx) + 4y0^2.

Set equal to |v|^2 ((t_mid - t_E)^2 + μ^2). Cancel μ^2 |v|^2:

|v|^2 t_mid^2 + 4y0(t_mid dy - μ dx) + 4y0^2 = |v|^2 (t_mid - t_E)^2.

Thus:

4y0(t_mid dy - μ dx) = |v|^2 ((t_mid - t_E)^2 - t_mid^2) - 4y0^2.

Compute ((t_mid - t_E)^2 - t_mid^2) = (t_mid^2 - 2 t_mid t_E + t_E^2) - t_mid^2 = -2 t_mid t_E + t_E^2 = t_E(t_E - 2 t_mid) = t_E(t_E - (t_E+t_F)) = -t_E t_F.

Thus:

4y0(t_mid dy - μ dx) = |v|^2 (-t_E t_F) - 4y0^2 = -t_E t_F |v|^2 - 4y0^2.

So:

t_mid dy - μ dx = - (t_E t_F |v|^2 + 4y0^2)/(4y0).

Thus μ dx = t_mid dy + (t_E t_F |v|^2 + 4y0^2)/(4y0).

Hence μ = [ t_mid dy + (t_E t_F |v|^2 + 4y0^2)/(4y0) ] / dx.

Now, we can compute O' = t_mid v + μ n.

Now, the line ℓ in translated coordinates is through H' = H - A = (dx, h_y - y0) with direction v. So ℓ: X = H' + s v.

We need to show that distance from O' to ℓ equals the radius R' = |O' - B'|.

The distance from O' to ℓ is |(O' - H') × v| / |v|.

Compute O' - H' = (t_mid v + μ n) - H' = t_mid v + μ n - (dx, h_y - y0). But H' = (dx, h_y - y0). Note that v = (dx, dy). So:

O' - H' = (t_mid dx - dx + μ dy, t_mid dy - (h_y - y0) - μ dx) = ( (t_mid - 1) dx + μ dy, t_mid dy - h_y + y0 - μ dx).

Now, cross product (2D determinant) with v:

(O' - H') × v = ((t_mid - 1) dx + μ dy) * dy - (t_mid dy - h_y + y0 - μ dx) * dx = (t_mid - 1) dx dy + μ dy^2 - t_mid dy dx + h_y dx - y0 dx + μ dx^2 = (t_mid - 1 - t_mid) dx dy + μ (dx^2+dy^2) + h_y dx - y0 dx = -dx dy + μ |v|^2 + (h_y - y0) dx.

Thus (O' - H') × v = μ |v|^2 - dx dy + (h_y - y0) dx.

Now, we also need to compute R'^2 = |O' - B'|^2 = |v|^2 (t_mid^2 + μ^2) + 4y0(t_mid dy - μ dx) + 4y0^2. But we can also use the fact that O' is circumcenter, so R'^2 = |O' - E'|^2 = |v|^2 ((t_mid - t_E)^2 + μ^2). This might be simpler.

Now, the tangency condition is: |(O' - H') × v|^2 = |v|^2 R'^2.

Thus we need to verify:

( μ |v|^2 - dx dy + (h_y - y0) dx )^2 = |v|^2 * |v|^2 ((t_mid - t_E)^2 + μ^2) = |v|^4 ((t_mid - t_E)^2 + μ^2).

This is still messy. However, we have expressions for t_E, t_F, t_mid, μ in terms of the original parameters. Perhaps we can find that the left side simplifies to |v|^4 ((t_mid - t_E)^2 + μ^2) using the relations we have. Let's try to compute these expressions in terms of the original parameters.

We have:

L = |v|^2 = dx^2+dy^2.
t_E = 2r(p+r)/L.
t_F = 2(d(p-x0)+r(p+r))/L.
t_mid = (t_E+t_F)/2 = [2r(p+r)+2(d(p-x0)+r(p+r))]/(2L) = [r(p+r)+d(p-x0)]/L? Wait, compute: t_E+t_F = 2r(p+r)/L + 2(d(p-x0)+r(p+r))/L = 2(2r(p+r)+d(p-x0))/L? Actually, t_E = 2r(p+r)/L, t_F = 2(d(p-x0)+r(p+r))/L = 2d(p-x0)/L + 2r(p+r)/L. So t_E+t_F = 4r(p+r)/L + 2d(p-x0)/L = (4r(p+r) + 2d(p-x0))/L. Then t_mid = (2r(p+r) + d(p-x0))/L.

Thus t_mid = (2r(p+r) + d(p-x0))/L.

Now, compute t_mid - t_E = (2r(p+r)+d(p-x0))/L - 2r(p+r)/L = d(p-x0)/L.

Thus (t_mid - t_E)^2 = d^2(p-x0)^2/L^2.

Now, also we have that t_E t_F = [2r(p+r)/L] * [2(d(p-x0)+r(p+r))/L] = 4r(p+r)(d(p-x0)+r(p+r))/L^2.

So t_E t_F = 4r(p+r)(d(p-x0)+r(p+r))/L^2.

Now, we need to compute μ. We have:

μ = [ t_mid dy + (t_E t_F L + 4y0^2)/(4y0) ] / dx.

Plug t_mid and t_E t_F L:

t_mid dy = dy * (2r(p+r)+d(p-x0))/L.

And t_E t_F L = 4r(p+r)(d(p-x0)+r(p+r))/L^2 * L = 4r(p+r)(d(p-x0)+r(p+r))/L.

So (t_E t_F L + 4y0^2)/(4y0) = [4r(p+r)(d(p-x0)+r(p+r))/L + 4y0^2]/(4y0) = [4r(p+r)(d(p-x0)+r(p+r))/L]/(4y0) + (4y0^2)/(4y0) = [r(p+r)(d(p-x0)+r(p+r))/(y0 L)] + y0.

Thus μ = [ dy * (2r(p+r)+d(p-x0))/L + r(p+r)(d(p-x0)+r(p+r))/(y0 L) + y0 ] / dx.

Now, also note that dy = y_P - y0 = -p(x0+r)/y0 - y0 = -(p(x0+r)+y0^2)/y0 = -(p(x0+r)+r^2-x0^2)/y0 = -( (x0+r)(p+r-x0) )/y0? Actually, p(x0+r)+r^2-x0^2 = p(x0+r)+(r-x0)(r+x0) = (r+x0)(p+r-x0). So dy = -(r+x0)(p+r-x0)/y0.

And also dx = p - x0.

Now, we also have h_y = p(d-p)/y_P = p(d-p) / ( -p(x0+r)/y0 ) = - (d-p) y0/(x0+r).

Thus h_y - y0 = - (d-p) y0/(x0+r) - y0 = -y0( (d-p)/(x0+r) + 1 ) = -y0( (d-p+x0+r)/(x0+r) ). But note that x0+r =? We'll keep.

Now, we need to compute the left side of the tangency condition: ( μ L - dx dy + (h_y - y0) dx )^2 = L^2 ((t_mid - t_E)^2 + μ^2). Since L is the same as |v|^2, and (t_mid - t_E)^2 = d^2(p-x0)^2/L^2.

So we need to show:

( μ L - dx dy + (h_y - y0) dx )^2 = L^2 ( d^2(p-x0)^2/L^2 + μ^2 ) = d^2(p-x0)^2 + L^2 μ^2.

Thus equivalently, we need to show that the square of μ L - dx dy + (h_y - y0) dx equals d^2(p-x0)^2 + L^2 μ^2.

If we denote A = μ L - dx dy + (h_y - y0) dx, then we need A^2 = d^2(p-x0)^2 + L^2 μ^2.

This is reminiscent of the identity: (μ L - dx dy + (h_y - y0) dx)^2 = (μ L + (h_y - y0) dx)^2 - 2dx dy (μ L + (h_y - y0) dx) + (dx dy)^2? Not.

F = (x0 + t_F dx, y0 + t_F dy)

I'll define:

Let M = (0,0), N = (d,0). Let Ω: x^2+y^2 = r^2, Γ: (x-d)^2+y^2 = R^2, with r < R and |R-r| < d < R+r. Let A = (x0, y0) with y0 > 0, B = (x0, -y0). Then x0 = (d^2 - R^2 + r^2)/(2d), y0 = sqrt(r^2 - x0^2).

Set p = (d+R-r)/2. Then C = (-r,0), D = (d+R,0). The perpendicular bisector of CD is x = p, so P has x-coordinate p. From PA = PC, we get y_P = -p(x0+r)/y0. So P = (p, -p(x0+r)/y0).

Let v = (dx, dy) = (p - x0, y_P - y0). Then t_E = 2r(p+r)/|v|^2, t_F = 2(d(p-x0)+r(p+r))/|v|^2. So E = A + t_E v, F = A + t_F v.

H is orthocenter of triangle PMN. Since M and N lie on x-axis, the altitude from P is vertical x = p. The altitude from M is perpendicular to PN. PN vector = (d-p, -y_P). A direction perpendicular to PN is (y_P, d-p). So the altitude from M: parametric (0,0) + s(y_P, d-p). Setting x = p gives s = p/y_P. Then y = s(d-p) = p(d-p)/y_P. So H = (p, p(d-p)/y_P) = (p, - (d-p) y0/(x0+r)) since y_P = -p(x0+r)/y0.

Now, the circle (BEF): B = (x0, -y0). Let’s find its circumcenter O. We use the formula for circumcenter in terms of coordinates. Let’s denote:

L = |v|^2 = dx^2+dy^2.
Let t_mid = (t_E+t_F)/2 = (2r(p+r)+d(p-x0))/L = (2r(p+r)+d dx)/L.

Then we derived (after some algebra) that:

O = (x0 + t_mid dx + μ dy, y0 + t_mid dy - μ dx) where μ = [ t_mid dy + (t_E t_F L + 4y0^2)/(4y0) ] / dx.

But we also found the explicit expressions for o_x and o_y:

o_x = d ( x0 dx + y0 dy + L t_mid ) / dx + d dy t_E t_F / (4y0 dx)
o_y = d t_E t_F L / (4y0)

Now, we need to show that the line through H with direction v is tangent to circle (BEF). That is, the distance from O to ℓ equals the radius R = |O-B|.

The line ℓ passes through H and has direction v. So ℓ: X = H + s v.

The distance from O to ℓ is |(O-H) × v| / |v|.

We can compute (O-H) × v. Since O = (o_x, o_y), H = (p, h_y). So:

(O-H) × v = (o_x - p) dy - (o_y - h_y) dx.

Thus we need to verify:

[ (o_x - p) dy - (o_y - h_y) dx ]^2 = L ( (o_x - x0)^2 + (o_y + y0)^2 ) (since O-B = (o_x - x0, o_y - (-y0)? Actually B = (x0, -y0), so O-B = (o_x - x0, o_y + y0). So |O-B|^2 = (o_x - x0)^2 + (o_y + y0)^2.

Now, we substitute the expressions for o_x, o_y, dx, dy, p, h_y.

We have:

p = (d+R-r)/2.
x0 = (d^2 - R^2 + r^2)/(2d).
y0 = sqrt(r^2 - x0^2).
dx = p - x0.
dy = -p(x0+r)/y0 - y0 = -(p(x0+r)+y0^2)/y0 = -(p(x0+r)+r^2 - x0^2)/y0 = -( (x0+r)(p+r-x0) )/y0? Actually, p(x0+r)+r^2-x0^2 = p(x0+r)+(r-x0)(r+x0) = (r+x0)(p+r-x0). So dy = -(r+x0)(p+r-x0)/y0.

Now, we need to compute the needed expression.

Given the complexity, we can use the condition that P is circumcenter of ACD. That gives:

p C_x = |C|^2/2 and p D_x = |D|^2/2. Using C = (-r,0), D = (d+R,0), we have:

p (-r) = (r^2)/2? No, |C|^2 = r^2? Actually, |C|^2 = (-r)^2+0^2 = r^2. So p * (-r) = r^2/2 => -p r = r^2/2 => p = -r/2, which would force r=0. So that's not right. Wait, the condition is P·C = |C|^2/2, but P = (p, y_P) and C = (-r,0). So P·C = p*(-r) + y_P*0 = -p r. And |C|^2 = r^2. So -p r = r^2/2 => p = -r/2. But p is defined as (d+R-r)/2, which is positive. So this would imply r=0, which is not the case. So there is a mistake: The condition for circumcenter of triangle ACD: For points A, C, D, the circumcenter P satisfies |P-A| = |P-C| = |P-D|. That gives two equations: (P-A)·(P-A) = (P-C)·(P-C) and (P-A)·(P-A) = (P-D)·(P-D). This is not the same as P·C = |C|^2/2. The formula P·C = |C|^2/2 comes from the condition that the circumcenter of triangle with vertices 0, C, D is given by (c d (c̄ - d̄))/(c d̄ - c̄ d). But we set A as origin? Wait, in our coordinate system, A is not at origin. The circumcenter condition for triangle ACD does not give a linear equation in P; it gives quadratic equations. So the formula P·C = |C|^2/2 is only valid when A is at origin? Actually, if we translate so that A becomes origin, then the circumcenter of triangle ACD becomes the circumcenter of triangle with vertices 0, C', D', where C' = C-A, D' = D-A. Then the circumcenter P' satisfies P'·C' = |C'|^2/2 and P'·D' = |D'|^2/2. So in the translated system, we have those linear equations. But in our original system, we have A not at origin. So we should translate to A as origin. Let's do that.

Define new coordinates: X' = X - A. Then:

A' = (0,0)
C' = C - A = (-r - x0, -y0)
D' = D - A = (d+R - x0, -y0)
P' = P - A = (p - x0, y_P - y0) = (dx, dy)

The condition that P is circumcenter of ACD gives:

|P'|^2 = |P' - C'|^2 = |P' - D'|^2.

From |P'|^2 = |P' - C'|^2, we get P'·C' = |C'|^2/2. Similarly, P'·D' = |D'|^2/2.

Thus we have:
